Marriage of the Hadina and Its Effect on Custody
Ruling on when a hadina's marriage forfeits her custody right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a hadina's marriage forfeits her right to custody and explain the underlying 'illa based on the hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
Marriage to a non-mahram forfeits custody; marriage to a mahram preserves it, because only a non-mahram husband harms the child.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on a ruling from Al-Wiqayah: a custodial mother or grandmother loses her right to custody if she marries a man who is not a mahram to the child, but not if she marries a mahram. The commentator explains the reasoning by linking the ruling to the prophetic hadith and a qiyas based on the child's welfare. We will unpack the legal logic and the dispute over whether custody is the right of the hadina or the child.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Locate the two-part ruling: if the hadina marries a non-mahram, her right falls; if she marries a mahram, it does not. Note that this applies to any hadina, not just the mother.
Examples given: mother marrying the child's paternal uncle, grandmother marrying the child's grandfather.
2
Find the primary evidence (hadith)
The commentator quotes the hadith 'ma lam tankihi' (as long as you do not marry). This hadith is the basis for the rule that marriage can end custody. Identify that the hadith is general ('amm) and does not distinguish between mahram and non-mahram.
The hadith is from the previously mentioned context: the Prophet told a woman seeking custody, 'you have more right as long as you do not marry'.
3
Trace the qiyas reasoning that restricts the general hadith
The commentator explains that the 'illa (effective cause) for losing custody is the harm to the child because the new husband dislikes him. This 'illa is absent when the husband is a mahram, who naturally feels compassion. Hence, the general hadith is restricted through qiyas.
This is a case of takhsis al-amm by qiyas, accepted by Hanafi usul.
4
Analyse the dispute: is custody the right of the hadina or the child?
The commentary notes a disagreement: the well-known view (mashhur) among the majority is that custody is the hadina's right ('her right'). Another view says it is the child's right. Consider the practical consequence: if it is the child's right, the hadina cannot forfeit it by her actions.
The text does not resolve this dispute but states the mashhur view. The mufta bihi is that marriage to a non-mahram forfeits her right, consistent with the dominant view.
5
Note the limitation on mahram: lineage only
The commentator explicitly states that only mahram by lineage counts; a mahram by breastfeeding (e.g., uncle by rada') is treated like a non-mahram. This is confirmed by the consensus that a foster mother has no custody right.
This is a Hanafi specific position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطلاق باب ثبوت النسب والحضانة ۵۲۷ وبنكاح غيرِ مَحرَم منه يسقط حقها ، وبَمَحْرَمٍ لا كام نكحت عمه، وجدة جده (وبنكاح" غيرِ مَحرَم منه يسقط حقها ) : أي في الحضانة (١). وبمحرم لا كام نكحت عمه ، وجدة جده) : أي جدة نكحت جده [۱) قوله : وبنكاح ... الخ ؛ متعلق بقوله : الآتي يسقط»، وحاصله : إنّ الحاضنة أُماً كانت أو غيرها إذا نكحت من ليس بمحرم للصبي سقط حقها ؛ لأنَّ زوجها الأجنبي لا يحبه بل يبغضه، فلا يكون في ترك الصبي عندها نظر له ؛ ولذا قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في الحديث السابق ذكره : «ما لم تنكحي»، ولعلّك تفطّنت من هاهنا أنّ الضمير في قوله : «منه» راجع إلى «الطفل»، وفي إطلاق النكاح إشارة إلى أن مجرد النكاح مسقط للحق وإن لم يدخل الزوج الثاني بها. [۲] قوله : حقها ؛ أشار به إلى أنّ الحضانة حق الحاضنة، وهو المشهور بين الجمهور، وقيل: هو حق الطفل، وإلى عموم الحكم في كل حاضنة والدة كانت أو غيرها. (۳) قوله : وبمحرم ؛ أي بنكاحها المحرم منه لا يسقط حقها، والمراد به المحرم النسبي، فإنّ المحرم الرضاعي كالعم الرضاعي في هذا الباب كالأجنبي؛ ولذا لم يثبت حق الحضانة للأم الرضاعية إجماعاً. فإن قلت : حديث : «ما لم تنكحي» مطلق يفيد سقوط حقها بنكاحها من غير تقييد بنكاح المحرم ونكاح غيره، فما بالكم فرقتم بينهما. قلت : الحكم المذكور في الحديث معلول بإجماع المجتهدين القائسين، فإن النكاح لم يكن مسقطاً لحقها في تربية الطفل إلا شفقة على الطفل ؛ لكون الزوج الثاني لا ينظرُ إليه بنظر الرحمة، وهذا المعنى مفقود فيما إذا كان الزوج الثاني محرماً له ، فإنّ نظره عليه نظر شفقة ، فلا يكون النكاح به مسقطاً لحقها. [٤] قوله : كأم ؛ يعني نكحت أمّ الطفل بعد موت أبيه أو طلاقه عم الطفل يعني أخ زوجها الأول، فإنّ العم محرم للطفل، وكذا الجدّة إذا كان زوجها جد الطفل. (۱) لحصول الضرر للصغير، فإن زوج الأم ينظر إليه شَزْراً ، وينفق عليه نَزْراً، ويتبرم بمكانه ضرراً، فلا نظر في الدفع إليها خطرا . ينظر : فتح باب العناية) (٢ : ١٨٤).
Divorce: Chapter on Establishment of Lineage and Custody. And by marriage to a non-mahram of him, her right falls, and by a mahram it does not, like if she marries his uncle, and a grandmother married his grandfather. ('And by marriage to a non-mahram of him, her right falls'): i.e., in custody. ('And by a mahram it does not, like if she marries his uncle, and a grandmother married his grandfather'): i.e., a grandmother married his grandfather. [Footnote 1] He said: 'And by marriage... etc.' is related to his later statement 'falls'. Its summary: The hadina, whether mother or other, if she marries someone who is not a mahram to the boy, her right falls because her alien husband does not love him but rather hates him, so there is no benefit in leaving the boy with her. Hence the Prophet (peace be upon him) said in the previously mentioned hadith: 'As long as you do not marry.' And you may deduce from here that the pronoun in 'minhu' refers to 'the child', and the absolute mention of marriage indicates that mere marriage causes the right to fall even if the second husband has not consummated the marriage. [Footnote 2] He said: 'Her right' indicates that custody is the right of the hadina, which is the well-known view among the majority, and it is said that it is the right of the child, and it indicates the generality of the ruling for every hadina, whether mother or other. [Footnote 3] He said: 'And by a mahram' means that by her marriage to a mahram of him, her right does not fall. What is meant is a mahram by lineage, for a mahram by breastfeeding like a paternal uncle by breastfeeding in this chapter is like an alien; therefore, the right of custody is not established for a breastfeeding mother by consensus. If you say: The hadith 'As long as you do not marry' is absolute, indicating the fall of her right by her marriage without restriction between marriage to a mahram and marriage to other than him, so how have you differentiated? I say: The ruling mentioned in the hadith is based on the consensus of the mujtahids who analogize, for marriage only caused the fall of her right in raising the child out of compassion for the child, because the second husband does not look at him with mercy. This meaning is absent when the second husband is a mahram to him, for his look upon him is a look of compassion, so marriage to him does not cause her right to fall. [Footnote 4] He said: 'Like a mother' means if the child's mother after the father's death or divorce marries the child's paternal uncle, i.e., the brother of the first husband, for the paternal uncle is a mahram to the child. Similarly, the grandmother if her husband is the child's grandfather. (Footnote 1: For harm befalls the young one, for the stepfather looks at him with an evil eye, spends on him sparingly, and is annoyed by his presence harmfully, so there is no benefit in entrusting him to her.)
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling in the matn of Al-Wiqayah. It states that a hadina (custodian) loses her right to custody if she marries a non-mahram to the child, but not if she marries a mahram. The commentator explains the reasoning: a non-mahram husband will dislike the child, harming the child's welfare, whereas a mahram husband will have compassion. The hadith 'ma lam tankihi' (as long as you do not marry) is interpreted generally, but the mujtahids have limited it through qiyas: the 'illa (effective cause) for the loss of right is the lack of compassion from the new husband, which is absent when the husband is a mahram. The commentary also notes a dispute: custody is generally considered the right of the hadina (mother or other), though some say it is the child's right. Mahram here means by lineage; a mahram by breastfeeding is treated like a non-mahram. Examples: if the mother marries the child's paternal uncle, or the grandmother marries the grandfather, these are mahram marriages and do not forfeit custody.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A person with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to lineage, marriage, or breastfeeding; in this context, a mahram by lineage to the child.
سقط الحق (suqut al-haqq): The forfeiture or loss of the right to custody due to a legal cause, such as marriage to a non-mahram.
حضانة (hadanah): Custody of a child, including physical care and upbringing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following marriages by a custodial mother does NOT cause her to lose her right to custody?
A) She marries the child's paternal uncle (her deceased husband's brother).
B) She marries a man who is unrelated to the child.
C) She marries a man who is a mahram by breastfeeding to the child.
D) She marries the child's maternal uncle (her own brother).

Key Takeaway
A hadina's right to custody falls if she marries a non-mahram to the child, but remains if she marries a mahram by lineage, based on the 'illa of harm to the child.

Talaq — Divorce

When Marriage Drops Custody Rights
Rulings on custody loss due to marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a custodian's right to custody drops due to marriage, explain the 'illa that distinguishes mahram from non-mahram, and apply the rule to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Marriage to a non-mahram drops custody; marriage to a mahram does not – the 'illa is lack of compassion.

Custody (hadana) is the right of a mother or other female guardian to raise a minor child. But what happens when the custodian remarries? The Hanafi school, relying on hadith and reasoning, draws a clear line: marriage to a stranger (non-mahram) forfeits custody, while marriage to a mahram relative preserves it. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the underlying cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the opening lines: 'And by marriage to a non-mahram of him, her right drops, and by a mahram, no.' The ruling is that only marriage to a non-mahram (a stranger to the child) removes the custodian's right.
The ruling applies to any custodian, not just the mother.
2
Examine the proof and 'illa
The text cites the hadith 'as long as you do not marry' and explains that the reason (''illa) for the drop is that a non-mahram husband lacks compassion for the child. A mahram husband, being a blood relative, has natural compassion.
The 'illa is derived by ijtihad and is used to limit the general hadith.
3
Understand the return of the right
The passage states: 'The right returns with the removal of the marriage by which it dropped.' When the marriage ends (by divorce or death), the obstacle is gone and custody is restored.
No additional conditions are mentioned; the return is automatic.
4
Learn the order of asabah
If no female custodian is available, custody passes to male agnatic heirs (asabah) in the same order as inheritance: father, grandfather, full brother, half-brother, etc.
This is a backup rule when all female custodians are absent or disqualified.
5
Apply the 'illa to new cases
Test your understanding: Would a foster uncle (who is a mahram through breastfeeding) preserve custody? The text explicitly says foster mahram is like a foreigner, so the right drops. This shows the 'illa is based on blood relationship, not only legal prohibition of marriage.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.

Divorce. Chapter on Establishing Lineage and Custody. And by marriage to a non-mahram of him, her right drops, and by a mahram, no, like when she marries his uncle, and a grandmother marries his grandfather. (And by marriage to a non-mahram of him, her right drops): meaning in custody. And by a mahram, no, like when she marries his uncle, and a grandmother marries his grandfather). Then the scholars said: and by marriage to a non-mahram of him, her right drops. And by a mahram, no, like when she marries his uncle, and a grandmother marries his grandfather. Then the scholars said: And he said: 'and by marriage...' etc.; attached to his saying: 'drops'. Summary: indeed the custodian, mother or otherwise, if she marries someone who is not a mahram to the child, her right drops; because her foreign husband does not love him but rather hates him, so there is no consideration for the child being left with her. Hence the Prophet (peace be upon him) said in the aforementioned hadith: 'as long as you do not marry'. And perhaps you realized from here that the pronoun in his saying 'of him' refers to the child, and in the absolute marriage there is an indication that mere marriage drops the right even if the second husband has not consummated with her. [2] His saying: 'her right' indicates that custody is the right of the custodial mother, and this is well-known among the majority, and it was said: it is the right of the child, and it indicates the generality of the ruling for every custodian, mother or other. (3) His saying: 'and by a mahram' means by her marriage to a mahram of him, her right does not drop, and the intended meaning is the relative mahram, for the foster mahram like the foster uncle in this chapter is like a foreigner; hence the right of custody is not established for the foster mother by consensus. If you say: the hadith 'as long as you do not marry' is absolute, indicating that her right drops by her marriage without restriction to marriage to a mahram or other. Then why have you differentiated between them? I say: the ruling mentioned in the hadith is reasoned by the consensus of the jurists who practice analogy, for marriage did not drop her right to raise the child except out of compassion for the child; because the second husband does not look upon him with mercy. This meaning is absent when the second husband is a mahram to him, for his look upon him is a look of compassion, so marriage to him does not drop her right. [4] His saying: 'like a mother' means the child's mother marries after the death of his father or his divorce the child's uncle, meaning the brother of her first husband, for the uncle is a mahram to the child. Likewise the grandmother if her husband is the child's grandfather. (1) Because of harm to the young child, for the mother's husband looks at him sideways, and spends on him sparingly, and is annoyed by his presence harmfully, so there is no consideration in handing him over to her. Look: Fath Bab al-Inaya (2:184). The right returns with the removal of the marriage by which it dropped. Then the asabah according to their order. Then he said: so this is from the chapter of conjunction on two different agents, and the genitive is advanced. (And the right returns with the removal of the marriage by which it dropped. Then the asabah according to their order.) [1] His saying: 'so this' means that the author conjoined his saying 'grandmother' to his saying 'mother', and his saying 'her grandfather' to his saying 'her uncle', thus conjoining two words on two different agents: one is the genitive particle and the second is the accusative, which is permissible among grammarians if the genitive is advanced. [2] His saying: 'and returns...' etc.; meaning if the marriage by which the custodian's right dropped, like marriage to a foreigner, ends, by divorce or death of the husband, her dropped right returns because the obstacle has ceased. [3] His saying: 'then the asabah' plural of 'asaba with fatha – and it is originally the plural of 'asib from 'asaba al-qawm bi-fulan, they surrounded him, and 'usuba with damm is the verbal noun. And the term 'asaba, although originally a plural like talaba plural of talib, has come to be used as a common noun, so it is used for one, two, and more. They defined 'asaba as every heir who takes all the property when alone and takes what remains of the fixed shares when fixed share holders exist. Then the 'asaba is divided into two types: relative and causal. If the 'asaba is a relative to the one for whom he is 'asaba, it is the first, otherwise it is the second, which is the patron of emancipation; i.e., the emancipator with kasr of the ta, for whoever frees a male or female slave, his wala' belongs to him, and he inherits from him if he has no other among the fixed share holders and relative 'asaba. And let the detail of these topics be sought from the books of inheritance. [4] His saying: 'according to their order' means according to the order mentioned in the chapter of inheritance considered in their inheritance, so the father is prioritized, then the grandfather (i.e., father's father even if higher), then the full brother, then the half-brother, then sons of the full brother, then sons of the half-brother. (1) That is, if there is no woman deserving of custody, then the right is for the 'asaba according to their order in inheritance: the father is prioritized, then the grandfather, then the full brother, then the half-brother, then their sons likewise, then the uncle, then his son. See: Majma' al-Anhur (1:482).
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya is a commentary expanding on the rulings of custody (hadana). The author opens by stating the key rule: when a custodian (mother or otherwise) marries someone who is not a mahram to the child, her right to custody drops. This is based on the well-known hadith 'as long as you do not marry' and on the reasoning that an unrelated stepfather does not have paternal affection for the child. Even if the marriage is not consummated, the right drops immediately upon marriage. The commentator clarifies that the pronoun 'of him' refers to the child, not the woman. There is a dispute about whether custody is the right of the mother or the child; the majority view is that it is the mother's right. However, if the woman marries a mahram of the child (e.g., the child's uncle or grandfather), her right does not drop, because a mahram relative naturally cares for the child. The commentator addresses a possible objection from the general wording of the hadith and rebuts it using istihsan (juristic preference) by citing the 'illa (effective cause): the loss of compassion. Since a mahram husband has compassion, the 'illa is absent. The right that dropped due to marriage is restored when that marriage ends by divorce or death, because the obstacle is removed. Finally, if no female custodian is available, the right passes to the 'asaba (male agnatic heirs) in order of inheritance priority: father, grandfather, full brother, half-brother, their sons, uncle, etc. The passage ends with a grammatical note on conjoining.

Key Terms
الحضانة (al-hadana): The right of a female guardian to raise and care for a minor child in her custody.
محرم (mahram): A person who is permanently prohibited to marry the child due to blood relationship or fosterage (but foster mahram is treated as foreign in this context).
عصبة (asaba): Male agnatic heir who inherits the entire estate or the residue after fixed shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a mother's right to custody return after it is lost due to marriage?
A) Immediately upon marriage.
B) Only if she divorces her new husband.
C) When the marriage that caused the loss ends, whether by divorce or death.
D) It never returns.

Key Takeaway
Custody drops only when a custodian marries a non-mahram, and returns when that marriage ends.

Return of Custody Right and Restrictions on 'Asaba Custodians
Commentary on custody return and 'asaba restrictions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a mother's forfeited custody right returns and identify which 'asaba may not take custody of a female child, applying the mahram and fasiq exceptions.

Core Idea
A female child's custody may be denied to a non-mahram or fasiq 'asaba due to potential temptation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah unpacks a key custody rule: when a mother loses custody by marrying a non-mahram, her right returns the moment that marriage ends. Then, it explains that 'asaba relatives have custody in order of inheritance, but with two critical exceptions for female children—they must be a mahram and not a fasiq. The commentator also clarifies that a young infant is not subject to this restriction. Understanding these conditions prevents misapplication of custody rights.

Guided Path
1
Trace the return of the mother's right
The mother's custody right falls when she marries a non-mahram (ghayr mahram). When that marriage ends (divorce or death), her right automatically returns because the impediment is removed.
This is a direct rule from the matn, explained by the commentator.
2
Define 'asaba and their order
The 'asaba are agnatic heirs who take the entire inheritance when alone or the residue after fixed shares. In custody, they follow the same order as in inheritance: father, grandfather, then brothers and their sons, then uncles and their sons.
The text distinguishes lineage 'asaba from causal 'asaba (mawla of emancipation).
3
Identify the restriction for female children
Despite the general order, a female child (sabiyyah) must not be given to an 'asaba who is not a mahram (e.g., paternal cousin, former master) because of the possibility of fitna (temptation).
This is an exception introduced by 'lakin' (but).
4
Apply the fasiq disqualification
Even if the 'asaba is a mahram, if he is a fasiq (sinful) or particularly a fasiq majin (debauched), the female child is not given to him, as the risk of fitna is greater.
The same applies to a mother who is fajira (immoral) to the extent that she wastes the child.
5
Recognize the age nuance
The restriction applies only when the female child is 'mushatah' (desired, i.e., of an age where she could be subject to temptation). For a very young child (e.g., one-year-old), there is no fitna, so she may be given to a non-mahram.
This is the apparent reasoning from Kashf al-Rumuz and al-Bahr al-Raiq.

ويعود الحق بزوال نكاح سقط به. ثم العصبات على ترتيبهم.
ثم قال: ويعود...الخ؛ يعني إذا زال النكاح الذي سقط به حق الحاضنة كالنكاح بالأجنبي، بأن طلقها أو مات عنها، عاد حقها الساقط؛ لأن المانع قد زال. ثم العصبات؛ جمع عصبة - بفتحات -: وهو في الأصل جمع عاصب من عصب القوم بفلان أحاطوا به حوله، والعصوبة – بالضم – مصدر، وقد صار لفظ العصبة وإن كان في الأصل جمعاً كطلبة جمع طالب في استعمالهم بمنزلة اسم الجنس، فيطلق على الواحد وعلى الاثنين وعلى ما فوقهما. وعرفوا العصبة بكل وارث يحرز جميع المال إذا انفرد ويأخذ ما أبقته أصحاب الفرائض عند وجود أصحاب الفرائض. ثم العصبة على قسمين: نسبية، وسببية، فإن كانت العصبة ذا قرابة بمن هو عصبة فهو الأول وإلا فهو الثاني، وهو مولى العتاقة؛ أي المعتق – بكسر التاء –، فإن من أعتق عبداً أو أمةً كان ولاؤه له، وكان وارثه إذا لم يكن له غيره من أصحاب الفرائض والعصبات النسبية، وليطلب تفصيل هذه المباحث من كتب علم الفرائض. على ترتيبهم؛ أي على الترتيب المذكور في "بحث الفرائض" المعتبر في إرثهم، فيقدم الأب ثم الجد - أي أب الأب وإن علا -، ثم الأخ العيني، ثم الأخ لأب، ثم بنو الأخ لأب وأم، ثم بنو الأخ لأب. (١) أي إن لم توجد امرأة مستحقة للحضانة فالحق للعصبات على ترتيبهم في الإرث فيقدم الأب ثم الجد ثم الأخ لأب وأم ثم لأب ثم بنوه كذلك ثم العمّ ثم بنوه. ينظر: "مجمع الأنهر"(١ : ٤٨٢).
لكن لا تدفع صبية إلى عصبةٍ غيرِ مَحرَم كمولى العتاقة، وابن العم، ولا فاسق ماجن.
قال: لكن؛ استدراك مما فهم من إطلاق قوله: "ثم العصبات على ترتيبهم"، من أن العصبات كلهم ذوو حق حضانة الطفل، ذكراً كان أو أنثى. صبية؛ الحاصل أنه لا تدفع أنثى إلى من كان عصبة غير محرم منه، كابن العم من العصبات النسبية ومولى العتاقة من العصبات السببية؛ لاحتمال الفتنة، وأما الذكر فيدفع إلى مولى العتاقة وغيره؛ ولو كان مولى العتاقة امرأة تدفع الأنثى إليه دون الذكر. ولا؛ أي لو كان أحد ممن له حق الحضانة فاسقاً لا تدفع الصبية إليه، وإن كان محرماً لها، لا سيما إذا كان فاسقاً ماجناً، فإن احتمال الفتنة فيه أقوى وأكثر؛ ولذا صرحوا بأنها لا تدفع إلى الأم الفاجرة فجوراً يضيع به الولد. قال الخير الرملي: يشترط في الحاضنة أن تكون حرة عاقلة بالغة أمينة قادرة، وأن تخلو من زوج أجنبي، وكذا في الحاضن الذكر يشترط فيه سوى الشرط الأخير، هذا ما يؤخذ من كلامهم. الحيل؛ - بكسر الحاء المهملة، وفتح الياء المثناة التحتية، جمع حيلة – ; (١) وفي تقييده عدم الدفع إلى أحدهما بوجود محرم من ذوي الأرحام إشارة إلى أنه يدفع إلى أحدهما إن لم يوجد محرم منهم إذ لا اعتبار لمجرد احتمال الفساد حينئذ، والظاهر من التعليل أن محل عدم الدفع من كانت مشتهاة، وأما لو كانت غير مشتهاة كبنت سنة مثلا فلا مطلقا؛ لأنه لا فتنة. ينظر: كشف الرموز (١ : ٢٩٧). And the right returns with the removal of the marriage by which it was dropped. Then the asaba (agnatic heirs) according to their order. Then he said: 'And it returns...etc.' meaning: if the marriage by which the right of the custodian was dropped, such as marriage to a stranger, ends by divorce or death of the husband, her dropped right returns because the impediment has been removed. Then the asaba: plural of 'asaba - with fatha - originally plural of 'asib from 'asaba al-qawm bi-fulan (they surrounded him). It has become used as a generic noun, applied to one, two, or more. They defined the 'asaba as every heir who takes the entire property when alone and takes what remains of the fixed shares when there are fixed share holders. Then the 'asaba are of two types: by lineage and by cause. If the 'asaba is a relative, it is the first type; otherwise it is the second, which is the mawla of emancipation (the emancipator). For whoever emancipates a male or female slave, the wala' (patronage) belongs to him, and he inherits from the freed person if there is no other fixed share heirs or lineage asaba. The details of these topics should be sought from books on the science of inheritance. 'According to their order': meaning the order mentioned in the 'Section on Inheritance' considered in their inheritance: precedence is given to the father, then the grandfather (i.e., father's father even if high), then the full brother, then the consanguine brother, then the sons of the full brother, then the sons of the consanguine brother. (1) Meaning: if there is no woman entitled to custody, the right goes to the asaba according to their order in inheritance: precedence is given to the father, then the grandfather, then the full brother, then the consanguine brother, then his sons similarly, then the paternal uncle, then his sons. See: Majma' al-Anhur (1:482). But a female child is not given to a non-mahram asaba, such as the mawla of emancipation and the paternal cousin, nor to a sinful debauchee. He said: 'But' is an exception from what is understood from the unqualified statement 'then the asaba according to their order' that all asaba have the right to custody of the child, male or female. 'A female child': the result is that a female is not given to someone who is an asaba but not a mahram to her, like the paternal cousin from the lineage asaba and the mawla of emancipation from the causal asaba, due to the possibility of temptation. As for a male child, he is given to the mawla of emancipation and others; and if the mawla of emancipation is a woman, the female child is given to her, not the male. 'And not': meaning if one who has the right to custody is sinful, the female child is not given to him, even if he is a mahram, especially if he is a sinful debauchee, because the possibility of temptation is stronger and greater. Therefore they explicitly stated that she is not given to a mother who is immoral to the extent that she wastes the child. Al-Khayr al-Ramli said: The conditions for the female custodian are that she be free, sane, adult, trustworthy, capable, and free from a foreign husband. Likewise for the male custodian, the same conditions except the last one. This is what is taken from their statements. 'al-Hiyal': with kasra on ha, fatha on ya, plural of hila (trick). (1) And in his restriction of not giving the child to either of them when there is a mahram from among the relatives on the mother's side, there is indication that it is given to one of them if there is no mahram from them, because mere possibility of corruption is not considered then. And the apparent from the reasoning is that the case of non-giving is when the female is desired (mushatah). But if she is not desired, like a one-year-old girl, then it is given absolutely because there is no temptation. See: Kashf al-Rumuz (1:297). (2) (al-Bahr al-Raiq) (4:183). Prevented.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding custody rights. It addresses two main issues: first, the return of the mother's custody right when the marriage that caused its fall ends; second, the definition and order of 'asaba (agnatic heirs) in custody, with an important exception. The commentator explains that if a woman loses custody due to marrying a non-mahram, her right returns immediately upon divorce or death of that husband, since the impediment is removed. Then he defines 'asaba, distinguishing between lineage-based and cause-based asaba (the latter being the emancipator). The order of 'asaba in custody follows the order of inheritance: father, grandfather, then brothers and their sons, then uncles and their sons. However, a critical restriction is added: a female child must not be given to a non-mahram 'asaba, such as a paternal cousin or the former master, due to fear of fitna (temptation). Similarly, she must not be given to a fasiq (sinful) custodian, even if a mahram. The commentator further clarifies that for a male child, there is no such restriction. The conditions for a custodian are also mentioned: free, sane, adult, trustworthy, capable, and (for females) not married to a stranger. The apparent reasoning limits the prohibition to female children who are of an age where they may be desired; a very young child, like an infant, is not subject to this restriction.

Key Terms
عصبة ('asaba): An agnatic heir who inherits the entire estate when alone, or the residue after fixed shares. In custody, they are the father, grandfather, brothers, their sons, paternal uncles, etc.
محرم (mahram): A person with whom marriage is permanently forbidden due to blood relationship (e.g., father, brother), suckling, or marriage (e.g., father-in-law).
فاسق ماجن (fasiq majin): A sinful person who is also immoral or debauched, lacking religious and moral integrity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, under what condition may a female child be given to a non‑mahram 'asaba?
A) She may never be given to a non‑mahram 'asaba under any circumstances.
B) She may be given if the non‑mahram 'asaba is the only relative available.
C) She may be given if she is not of an age where she is desired (e.g., an infant).
D) She may be given if the non‑mahram 'asaba is a woman.

Key Takeaway
Custody of a female child is restricted to mahram and pious custodians; a non‑mahram or fasiq may be denied, but the restriction eases for very young children where no temptation exists.

Duration of Custody for a Boy and a Girl
Explaining duration of custody with age estimates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the duration of custody for a boy and a girl, identify the evidence for the boy's age of seven, and explain why the girl's custody ends at nine years (mufta bihi) due to corruption of the times.

Core Idea
Custody ends when a child becomes self-sufficient; for a boy at seven, for a girl at desirability (nine years) due to changing times.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah addresses the length of time a mother or grandmother retains custody of a child. The ruling differs for boys and girls, and within the Hanafi school there is an important shift from the earlier, more general ruling to a more specific estimate that accounts for social conditions. We will unpack the key ages, the evidence from hadith, and the reason the later view is preferred for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling for the boy
The text begins by stating that custody of a son lasts until he can independently eat, drink, dress, and clean himself. This is a functional test, not a fixed age.
The functional test leaves room for variation among children.
2
Understand the evidence for seven years
Al-Khassaf estimated this age as seven years, relying on the hadith 'Command your children to pray when they are seven' as evidence that by this age a boy is generally capable of self-care.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but indicative of ability.
3
Identify the two positions for the girl
The base text states custody until menstruation. But Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) narrates a different view: custody until the girl becomes desirable (madh al-shahwah), estimated at nine years.
Note that 'desirable' means she reaches an age of sexual attraction, not puberty.
4
Analyze why the later view is mufta bihi
Due to corruption of the times (fasad al-zaman), later scholars preferred Imam Muhammad's view to protect the girl from potential harm, thus custody ends at nine years for a girl (mufta bihi).
This is an example of istihsan overriding the literal ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأم والجدة أحق بالابن حتى يأكل ويشرب ويلبس، ويستنجي وحده : قَدَّره الخصاف بسبع سنين ، وعليه الفتوى). ويشكل عليه أنه قد ثبت التخيير عن أبي هريرة له عند ابن حبان ، وعن عند عبد الرزاق ، وعن علي الله عند الشافعي ، ولعلّ الحق هو القولُ بجواز التخيير لكن لا مطلقاً، بل بشرط أن يكون المظنون اختيار الطفل الأرفق الانظر وإلا فلا. [١] قوله : والأم....الخ ؛ شروع في بيان مدة الحضانة، وهي مختلفة باختلاف الذكرِ والأنثى، وظاهرُ كلامه يحكم بأنّ المدة المذكورة لحضانة الذكر مختصة بما إذا كانت الحاضنة أماً أو جدة لأب أو لأم وليس كذلك، بل هذه المدة في كل حاضنة، صرّح به «الدر المختار» وغيره، وإنما التفرقة ما إذا كانت الحاضنة إما أو جدة، وبين ما إذا كانت غيرهما في حضانة الأنثى.
[٢] قوله : وحده ؛ قيد لجميع الأفعال المذكورة، والحاصل : إنّ الحضانة للصغير غايتها أن يستغني عن خدمةِ النِّساء، فإن بعد ذلك لا يحتاج إلى كونه معهن، بل إلى التأديب والتعليم، والرجال أحقاء به، وذلك إنما يعرف بأن يقدر على أن يأكل وحده ويشرب ويلبس وينام ويستنجي. وبالجملة : يقدر على أن يفعل هذه الأفعال ونحوها من غير معين، بأن يبلغ سنّاً يقدر فيه الصبي غالباً على هذه الأفعال ولا يقدح فيه عدم قدرة صبي عليها لمرض.
[٣] قوله : قدره ؛ يشير إلى أنّ في ظاهر الرواية لم يقدّر سن توجد فيه هذه الغاية، بل ترك الحكم مطلقاً، بناءً على أنّ القدرة على هذه الأفعال، والاستغناء عن النِّساءِ يختلف وجوده بحسب اختلاف طبائع الصبيان. والخصاف له قدره بسبع سنين ، واعتمد عليه المشايخ ؛ نظراً إلى أن الغالب في هذا السن هو القدرة، ويؤخذ ذلك من حديث: مروا صبيانكم بالصلاة إذا بلغوا سبعاً)، فإنّ الأمر بها لا يكون إلا بعد القدرة على تمام الطهارة وغيره. والخصاف - بفتح الخاء المعجمة، وتشديد الصاد المهملة – لقب به أحمد بن عمرو، قيل في اسم أبيه : عمرو بن مهير من كبار مشايخ الحنفية، لقب به لأنه كان يأكل من كسب يده، كذا ذكره العَيْنِي في «البناية»، وقد فرغنا عن ترجمته في «مقدمة هذا التعليق»، وفي مقدّمة الهداية»، وفي «الفوائد البهية)، وغيرها من تأليفاتنا.
[١]قوله : وبالبنت ؛ عطف على قوله : «بالابن»، يعني الأم والجدة أحق بالأنثى إلى أن تبلغ فتحيض، وإن زاد عمرها على عشرة ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الأنثى بعدما تستغني في أفعالها الذاتية كالشرب والأكل وغيرهما عن خدمة النساء، تحتاج إليهن في تعلم الغزلِ والخياطة وغيرهمــا مــن آداب النساء، فتترك إلى أن تبلغ ، فحينئذ الحاجة إلى المعلم والمحافظ أكثر. [٢] قوله : حتى تشتهي ؛ يعني في رواية عن محمد الله : الأم والجدة أحق بالبنتِ حتى تبلغ مبلغ الشهوة، وقدروه بتسع سنين، وبه يفتى، كما في «الدر المختار»، وغيره.
[٣] قوله : وهو المعتمد...الخ ؛ أفتى المشايخ في هذه المسألة على خلاف ظاهرِ الرواية ؛ لفسادِ الزمان واختلافِ طبائع الإنسان، ففي بقاء الأنثى عند النساء بعد كونها مشتهاة احتمال الفسادِ في هذه الأزمان، فلا يعتبر بتقدير البلوغ.
The mother and the grandmother are more entitled to the son until he eats, drinks, dresses, and cleans himself alone. Al-Khassaf estimated it as seven years, and the fatwa is upon this. It is objected that the choice (takhyeer) is established from Abu Hurairah at Ibn Hibban, from at Abd al-Razzaq, and from Ali at al-Shafi'i; perhaps the truth is permissibility of takhyeer, but not absolutely, rather on condition that it is probable that the child chooses the more beneficial – consider that; otherwise no. [1] His saying: 'And the mother... etc.'; commencement in explaining the duration of custody, and it differs according to male and female. The apparent meaning of his statement rules that the mentioned duration for male custody is specific when the custodian is a mother or grandmother on the father's or mother's side, but it is not so; rather this duration applies to every custodian, as declared in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others. The distinction is only between when the custodian is a mother or grandmother and when it is someone else in the custody of the female. [2] His saying: 'Alone'; a restriction for all the mentioned actions. The conclusion: The custody of the young child reaches its end when he becomes independent of women's service; after that he does not need to be with them, but rather needs discipline and education, and men are more suited to that. This is known by his ability to eat alone, drink, dress, sleep, and clean himself. In summary, he can do these and similar actions without assistance, by reaching an age at which the boy is generally able to do these actions, and it is not harmed by a boy's inability due to illness. [3] His saying: 'He estimated it'; indicates that in the apparent riwayah no specific age for this end was fixed, but the ruling was left absolute, based on the fact that the ability for these actions and independence from women varies according to the temperaments of boys. Al-Khassaf estimated it as seven years, and the scholars relied on it, considering that generally at this age ability exists. This is taken from the hadith: 'Command your children to pray when they reach seven years' – because the command to pray is only after ability for complete purification and other matters. Al-Khassaf – with a fatha on the kha' and tashdid on the sad – is the nickname of Ahmad ibn 'Amr, said that his father's name was 'Amr ibn Muhayir, one of the major Hanafi scholars. He was nicknamed thus because he used to eat from the earnings of his hand. So mentioned by al-'Ayni in al-Binayah. And we have completed his biography in the introduction to this commentary, and in the introduction to al-Hidayah, and in al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyyah, and other of our writings. [1] His saying: 'And with the daughter'; linked to his saying 'with the son', meaning that the mother and grandmother are more entitled to the female until she reaches menstruation, even if her age exceeds ten. This is because after the female becomes independent in her intrinsic actions like drinking and eating and others from women's service, she needs them in learning spinning and sewing and other feminine arts, so she is left with them until she reaches [menstruation]; then the need for a teacher and protector is greater. [2] His saying: 'until she becomes desirable'; meaning in a narration from Muhammad: the mother and grandmother are more entitled to the girl until she reaches the age of desire, and they estimated it as nine years. And by this fatwa is given, as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others. [3] His saying: 'And it is the relied-upon... etc.'; The scholars gave fatwa in this matter contrary to the apparent riwayah due to the corruption of the times and the variation in human nature; for in the girl remaining with women after she has become desirable there is the possibility of corruption in these times, so the estimation of puberty is not considered.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the duration of custody (hadanah) for a boy and a girl. The base text states that the mother and grandmother are more entitled to the son until he can independently perform basic actions like eating, drinking, dressing, and cleaning himself. Al-Khassaf fixed this age at seven years, and this is the operative position (mufta bihi). The commentator clarifies that this duration is not limited to the mother or grandmother but applies to any custodian. For the daughter, the base text says custody lasts until she menstruates. However, in a narration from Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani), she remains until she becomes desirable (i.e., reaches an age where she is sexually attractive), estimated at nine years. Due to the corruption of the times, the scholars have relied on Imam Muhammad's view, as it better protects the girl from potential corruption. The commentator also notes that the hadith about commanding children to pray at age seven is taken as evidence for the typical age of independence. The passage also includes a brief biography of Al-Khassaf, a major Hanafi scholar.

Key Terms
الحضانة (al-hadanah): The right of a custodian (usually the mother or grandmother) to care for a young child in their home.
التخيير (al-takhyeer): The child's right to choose which parent to live with after a certain age (mentioned in some schools).
الخصاف (al-Khassaf): Ahmad ibn 'Amr al-Khassaf (d. 261/874), a major Hanafi jurist who estimated the custody age for a boy as seven years.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in this passage, when does custody of a girl end?
A) At the age of seven years
B) At menstruation (حيض)
C) When she becomes desirable (تُشْتَهى), around nine years
D) When she reaches puberty (بلوغ)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school estimates custody ends for a boy at seven years (self-sufficiency) and for a girl at nine years (desirability) due to changing social conditions, overriding the apparent riwayah of menstruation.

Custody of Daughter: Until Menstruation or Until Desired?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on daughter's custody duration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on when a daughter's custody transfers from mother to father, explain the rationale for each, and identify the mufta bihi position along with its justification using fasād al-zamān.

Core Idea
A daughter's custody ends when she becomes sexually desired (approx. age 9), not at puberty, due to corruption of the times.

In the Hanafi school, custody of a child is primarily the mother's right, but the duration of that right differs for sons and daughters. The classical texts report two positions on when the mother's custody over her daughter ends. This lesson examines the dispute between the three leading imams, the reasoning behind each view, and why later scholars departed from the apparent ruling to adopt a different criterion.

Guided Path
1
State the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah position
Identify the position attributed to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: custody ends when the daughter menstruates (reaches puberty). This is the apparent ruling of the narrations.
This view emphasizes the daughter's need for feminine education until self-sufficiency and then father's protection.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf
Note that Imam Muhammad narrates a different view: custody ends when the daughter becomes sexually desired (ishtihā'), estimated at nine years. This is a nawādir narration.
The difference is not between schools but within the Hanafi school itself.
3
Analyze the dalīl behind each view
Abu Hanifa focuses on the daughter's need to learn feminine skills from the mother until she is independent, then father's protection. Muhammad focuses on the risk of corruption once the girl becomes desirable, necessitating earlier transfer.
Both derive their reasoning from maqāṣid (objectives) of sharīʿah.
4
Understand the tarjīh mechanism
The commentator states that later scholars gave fatwa on Muhammad's view contrary to the ẓāhir al-riwāyah. This is justified by fasād al-zamān (corruption of the times) and differences in human nature.
This demonstrates how social change can influence which narration is preferred for fatwa.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilāf
Under Abu Hanifa's view, custody transfers at puberty (around 12-15). Under Muhammad's, it transfers at age nine. The mufta bihi is nine years.
This practical difference affects the lives of Hanafi Muslims significantly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالبنت حتى تحيض، وعن محمد الله حتى تُشْتَهى، وهو المعتمد لفساد الزمان. [1] قوله : وبالبنت؛ عطف على قوله : «بالابن»، يعني الأم والجدة أحق بالأنثى إلى أن تبلغ فتحيض، وإن زاد عمرها على عشرة؛ وذلك لأنَّ الأنثى بعدما تستغني في أفعالها الذاتية كالشرب والأكل وغيرهما عن خدمة النساء، تحتاج إليهن في تعلم الغزلِ والخياطة وغيرهما من آداب النساء، فتترك إلى أن تبلغ، فحينئذ الحاجة إلى المعلم والمحافظ أكثر. [2] قوله : حتى تشتهي؛ يعني في رواية عن محمد الله : الأم والجدة أحق بالبنتِ حتى تبلغ مبلغ الشهوة، وقدروه بتسع سنين، وبه يفتى، كما في «الدر المختار»، وغيره. [3] قوله : وهو المعتمد...الخ؛ أفتى المشايخ في هذه المسألة على خلاف ظاهرِ الرواية؛ لفسادِ الزمان واختلافِ طبائع الإنسان، ففي بقاء الأنثى عند النساء بعد كونها مشتهاة احتمال الفسادِ في هذه الأزمان، فلا يعتبر بتقدير البلوغ.
And with the daughter until she menstruates, and from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) until she is desired, and it is the relied-upon view due to the corruption of the times. [1] His statement: 'And with the daughter' is conjuncted to his statement 'with the son', meaning the mother and grandmother are more entitled to the female until she reaches puberty and menstruates, even if her age exceeds ten; because the female after she becomes independent in her essential actions like drinking and eating etc. from the service of women, needs them for learning spinning and sewing and other etiquettes of women, so she is left until she reaches puberty, then the need for a teacher and protector is greater. [2] His statement: 'until she is desired' means in a narration from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): the mother and grandmother are more entitled to the daughter until she reaches the age of desire, and they estimated it as nine years, and this is the fatwa, as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others. [3] His statement: 'and it is the relied-upon...etc.'; the scholars gave fatwa in this matter contrary to the apparent ruling of the narration due to corruption of the times and differences in human natures, for in the daughter staying with women after she is desired there is a possibility of corruption in these times, so consideration of the limit of puberty is not observed.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the duration of a mother's custody over her daughter. The base text states two positions: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that custody ends when the daughter menstruates (i.e., reaches puberty), while Imam Muhammad holds that custody continues until the daughter becomes sexually desirable (ishtihā'), which they estimated as nine years. The commentator explains that the relied-upon position for fatwa is Muhammad's view, due to the corruption of the times (fasād al-zamān). The reason is that once a girl becomes desirable, leaving her with women poses a risk of corruption, so the father's custody is preferred earlier. The practical consequence is that today, a daughter moves to her father's custody at around nine years old, not at puberty.

Key Terms
حتى تحيض (ḥattā taḥīḍ): Until she menstruates, i.e., reaches puberty (bulūgh).
حتى تشتهى (ḥattā tushtahā): Until she is desired sexually (ishtihā'), estimated at nine years of age.
المعتمد (al-muʿtamad): The relied-upon position for fatwa, as opposed to the apparent ruling of the narration.
فساد الزمان (fasād al-zamān): Corruption of the times; a justification for departing from the apparent ruling due to moral decline.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the daughter reaches puberty (menstruation)
B) When the daughter reaches the age of desire (estimated at nine years)
C) When the daughter marries
D) When the daughter reaches age fifteen

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a daughter's custody transfers from mother to father when she becomes sexually desired (estimated at nine years), not at puberty, due to the principle of fasād al-zamān.

Custody of Daughter by Other Female Relatives: Duration Until Desire
A ruling stating the agreed-upon limit of custody for female relatives other than mother and grandmother, with a note on internal disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the duration of custody for female custodians other than the mother and grandmother in the Hanafi school, and identify the muftā bihī position despite an internal ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
All female custodians retain custody of a daughter until the age of desire – this is the agreed, fatwa-worthy limit.

This passage clarifies the custody period for female relatives other than the mother and grandmother, such as the sister or maternal aunt. While the principle is agreed upon, there was a minor internal Hanafi dispute about the exact end point. Understanding this ruling is essential for determining when custody must be transferred to the father.

Guided Path
1
Identify the custodians
Note that this ruling applies to female custodians other than the mother and grandmother (ghayruhumā). These include full sister, maternal aunt, etc.
Hanafi ranking: after mother and grandmother come full sister, then maternal aunt.
2
Recognize the agreed limit
The passage states it is agreed (ittifāqī) that the daughter stays with these custodians until the time of desire (ishtihā').
Ishtihā' is the age at which a girl begins to attract sexual interest, determined by custom.
3
Understand the internal ikhtilaf
The passage notes that according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs), the period is a matter of dispute, but the muftā bihī (operative ruling) remains the same limit – ishtihā'.
The dispute does not affect the fatwa; the agreed limit is adopted for all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغيرهما حتى تشتهى. [4] قوله : وغيرهما؛ هذا اتفاقي؛ فتترك الأنثى عند غير الأم والجدة من الحاضنات إلى زمان الشهوة، وبعده لا بالاتفاق، وأما عندهما: فمدة التركِ اختلافي، وقد عرفت أنّ المفتى به هناك أيضاً هو اعتبار هذا الحد.
And others besides them until she is desired. [4] His statement: 'and others besides them'; this is agreed upon; so the female is left with the custodians other than the mother and grandmother until the time of desire, and after that there is no agreement. As for according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the period of leaving is a matter of ikhtilaf, and you have known that the muftā bihī there also is consideration of this limit.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for female custodians other than the mother and grandmother (ghayruhumā). These include the sister, maternal aunt, etc. The commentator explains that it is agreed (ittifāqī) that the daughter remains with them until she reaches the age of desire (ishtihā'). After that, there is disagreement (no agreement). As for the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the duration of custody for these custodians is disputed (ikhtilāfī) among them, but the muftā bihī here as well is the same limit (until ishtihā'). In practice, therefore, all female custodians keep the daughter until she becomes desirable, at which point custody transfers to the father or other male guardian.

Key Terms
غيرهما (ghayruhumā): Female custodians other than the mother and the grandmother (i.e., sister, aunt, etc.).
اتفاقي (ittifāqī): Agreed upon – the ruling that the daughter stays until ishtihā' is not disputed.
عندهما (indahumā): According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) – the two primary authorities besides Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The agreed-upon limit of custody for female custodians other than mother and grandmother is the daughter's age of desire (ishtihā'), and this is the operative fatwa despite internal Hanafi debate.

General Rule: Travel Only to Her Homeland
Ruling restricting travel to marriage homeland

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a divorced mother's travel with her child and identify the two conditions that must be met for permissible travel.

Core Idea
A divorced mother may only travel with her child to the place where she was married – two conditions: homeland and marriage location.

This passage from 'Umdat al‐Ri'āya presents the core rule regarding a divorced mother's custody travel. At first glance, the rule seems narrow: she cannot travel except to her original homeland. But the commentary reveals that this seemingly simple rule contains two critical conditions that protect the father's visitation rights and the child's welfare.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the act
The rule begins with a divorced woman (مطلقة) and the act of traveling (تسافر) with her child (بولدها). The child is in her custody (حضانة).
The mother must be irrevocably divorced (بائن) – this is clarified later in the passage.
2
State the general restriction
The matn says she must not travel except to her homeland (إلا إلى وطنها). The prohibition is general; the exception is specific.
The wording implies a strong prohibition (لا تسافر) – not recommended, but prohibited unless exceptions apply.
3
Identify the two conditions from the commentary
The commentary (شرح) explains that 'except to her homeland' requires two things: (1) the destination must be her homeland (وطنها), and (2) her marriage contract with the child's father must have taken place there.
This is not two separate conditions but conjunctive: 'the homeland in which she was married'.
4
Apply to a concrete scenario
Consider a mother who was married in Makkah but her original family lives in Madinah. She cannot take the child to Madinah permanently because Makkah is her 'homeland' for this ruling – the marriage location defines it.
This prevents the mother from moving the child away from the father's proximity just by relocating to a familiar place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولَا تُسَافِرُ مُطَلَّقَةٌ بِوَلَدِهَا إلاَّ إِلَى وَطَنِهَا الَّذِي نُكِحَتْ فِيهِ. [2] قوله : ولا تسافر...الخ؛ تفصيلُ هذه المسألة على ما في «الهداية» و«الكنز» وشروحهما : إنّه لا يجوز للمطلقة الخروج بولدها الذي هو في حضانتها من المصر إلى القرية، وإن كان بينهما تفاوت قليل؛ لاحتمال ضرر الولد، بتخلقه بأخلاق أهل السوء، ومن المصر إلى المصر. أو من القرية إلى المصر يجوز إن كان بينهما تفاوت قليل، بحيث يمكن للزوج أن يبصر ولده ثم يرجع في يومه؛ لأنه كانتقال من محلة مصر إلى محلة أخرى، وإن كان بينهما تفاوت كثير، سواءً كان مدّة السفر أو ما دونه فلا يجوز له، إلا إذا كان ما انتقلت إليه وطنها، وقد نكحها زوجها: أي أبو ولدها هناك. [3] قوله : مطلقة؛ أي بالطلاق البائن، فإنّ المطلقة الرجعية حكمها حكمُ المنكوحة، ليس لهما الخروج مطلقاً من بيت العدّة، وكذا المعتدة مطلقاً ليس لها الخروج قبل انقضاء العدة. كذا في «البحر». وقال الخير الرملي : الظاهرُ أنّ المتوفى عنها زوجها كالمطلقة في ذلك، فلا تملك ذلك بلا إذن الأولياء؛ لقيامهم مقام الأب وما فيه إضرار بالولد ظاهر المنع. [4] قوله : إلا إلى وطنها؛ ظاهره أنه لا بُدَّ من تحقق الأمرين، وهو أن يكون المنتقل إليه وطناً لها، وأن يكون وقع عقدها بوالد ولدها فيه، سواء كان ذلك مصراً أو قرية.
And a divorced woman must not travel with her child except to her homeland in which she was married. [2] His statement: 'and a divorced woman must not travel...etc.'; the detail of this issue as in al-Hidāyah, al-Kanz and their commentaries is: It is not permissible for a divorced woman to go out with her child who is in her custody from a city to a village, even if the difference between them is small, because of the possibility of harm to the child by his acquiring the morals of evil people. And from a city to a city, or from a village to a city, it is permissible if the difference between them is small, such that the husband can see his child and return the same day, because it is like moving from one district of a city to another district. But if the difference between them is great, whether it is a travel distance or less, then it is not permissible for her, except if what she moves to is her homeland, and her husband had married her there, i.e., the father of her child there. [3] His statement: 'divorced woman' means by irrevocable divorce (bā'in). For a revocable divorced woman, her ruling is that of a married woman; both are not allowed to leave the house of 'iddah at all. Similarly, a woman in 'iddah (from any cause) is not allowed to leave before the end of 'iddah. This is in al-Bahr. Al-Khayr al-Ramlī said: It appears that the widow is like the divorced in this matter, so she does not have that right without permission of the guardians because they stand in the place of the father, and any harm to the child is apparently prohibited. [4] His statement: 'except to her homeland' implies that two conditions must be fulfilled: that the place she moves to is her homeland, and that her marriage contract with the father of her child took place there, whether that place is a city or a village.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage lays out the conditions under which a divorced mother (and by extension a widow) with custody of her child can travel. The general rule is: she cannot travel with the child to a place distant from the father's residence, because it harms the father's right to visit the child. The key condition is that she may only travel to her original homeland (waṭan) where she was married to the child's father. The commentary provides details: traveling from a city to a village is prohibited even if the distance is small, due to risk of bad influence. Travel between two cities or from village to city is permitted if the distance is small enough that the father can visit and return in one day. If the distance is great (a travel distance or more), it is prohibited unless the destination is her homeland where she was married. The mother must be irrevocably divorced (bā'in); a revocably divorced woman (raj'ī) is still considered married and cannot leave the 'iddah home. Widows are treated similarly to irrevocably divorced women in this regard. The underlying reasoning is to protect the father's right of visitation and the child's welfare.

Key Terms
مطلقة (muṭallaqah): A woman who has been divorced - here specifically irrevocably divorced (bā'in).
وطنها (waṭanuhā): Her homeland (original place of residence) where the marriage contract with the child's father took place.
تفاوت (tafāwut): Difference (in distance) between two places; if small, father can visit and return same day; if large, it is travel distance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi matn, a divorced mother with custody may only travel with her child to:
A) Any city where her relatives live
B) The city where she was married to the child's father
C) Any city that is within a short travel distance
D) The city where the child's father currently resides

Key Takeaway
A divorced mother's travel with her child is prohibited unless to the specific location where her marriage contract with the father took place.

Custody of Female Child Until Age of Desire
Ruling on custody duration for non-mother custodians

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the agreed Hanafi ruling on the custody duration of a female child with non-mother custodians and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute after the age of shahwa.

Core Idea
Custody of a girl with other custodians ends at shahwa; after that, Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn differ.

This lesson examines a key ruling from the Hanafi school on child custody: the duration a female child remains with custodians other than her mother and grandmother. The text presents an agreed position that custody lasts until she reaches the age of sexual desire (shahwa), but then notes a disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding what happens after that point. Understanding this dispute clarifies the practical limits of custody rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the custodians in question
The passage refers to 'other than them' (غيرهما), meaning custodians other than the mother and grandmother, such as aunts, sisters, etc. These are the female custodians (حاضنات) in the Hanafi order after the mother and grandmother.
The mother and grandmother have longer custody rights; this passage specifically addresses the others.
2
State the agreed ruling
There is consensus (اتفاقي) among Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn that these custodians keep the female child until the time of shahwa (desire for marriage). Shahwa is understood as the age when she becomes marriageable, typically around puberty.
'Agreed' here means all three agreed that custody ends at shahwa; the disagreement is about custody after that point.
3
Identify the post-shahwa disagreement
After shahwa, Abu Hanifa's view differs from the Sahibayn. According to the text, 'after it there is no agreement' (وبعده لا بالاتفاق). The passage notes that the duration of leaving (i.e., custody) after shahwa is a matter of dispute according to the Sahibayn.
The commentary implies Abu Hanifa holds that the girl may choose her custodian after shahwa, while the Sahibayn dispute whether custody continues further.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states that the relied-upon position (المفتى به) is the consideration of this limit of shahwa. That means the operative ruling for fatwa is that custody with these custodians ends at shahwa.
Even though the Sahibayn disputed the duration after shahwa, the tarjih (preference) is to adhere to the limit of shahwa.
5
Apply to a scenario
Example: A 12-year-old girl reaches puberty (shahwa). Her maternal aunt had custody. According to the mufta bihi, the aunt’s custody ends at that moment. The girl then may have the right to choose her guardian, or custody may transfer to another, depending on further rulings.
This clarifies that the girl is not forced to stay with the same custodian indefinitely; the limit protects her interests as she approaches marriageable age.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب ثبوت النسب والحضانة
(وغيرهما حتى تشتهى) : أي غير الأم والجدة أحق بالبنتِ حتى تشتهى.
قوله : وغيرهما ؛ هذا اتفاقي ؛ فتترك الأنثى عند غير الأم والجدة من الحاضنات إلى زمان الشهوة، وبعده لا بالاتفاق، وأما عندهما : فمدة التركِ اختلافي، وقد عرفت أنّ المفتى به هناك أيضاً هو اعتبار هذا الحد.
The Book of Divorce, Chapter on Establishing Lineage and Custody. (And other than them until she desires): meaning other than the mother and grandmother are more entitled to the daughter until she desires. He said: 'And other than them'; this is agreed upon; so the female is left with other than the mother and grandmother from the custodians until the time of desire, and after it there is no agreement. But according to them (the two disciples), the duration of leaving is disputed, and you know that the fatwa there also is the consideration of this limit.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the custody of a female child from the chapter on establishing lineage and custody in the Book of Divorce. The matn states: 'And other than them (mother and grandmother) have custody of the daughter until she desires (marriage).' The commentary clarifies that 'other than them' refers to other female custodians (like aunts, sisters, etc.). The ruling is agreed upon (ittifaqi) between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, that these custodians retain custody of the girl until she reaches the age of sexual desire (shahwa), i.e., when she becomes sexually mature and capable of marriage. After that period, there is no agreement: according to Abu Hanifa, the girl is then free to choose, but according to the two disciples, the duration of custody with these custodians is a matter of difference. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the consideration of this limit (the time of desire) as stated in the text.

Key Terms
شهوة (shahwa): Sexual desire or the age when a girl becomes marriageable, typically puberty.
حاضنات (hāḍināt): Female custodians eligible to raise a child, such as mother, grandmother, aunts, sisters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the agreed ruling for custodians other than the mother and grandmother regarding a female child?
A) Custody until she reaches shahwa
B) Custody until she gets married
C) Custody until she is weaned
D) Custody until she reaches age 18

Key Takeaway
The agreed Hanafi ruling is that non-mother custodians retain custody of a female child only until she reaches shahwa; the mufta bihi considers this limit binding.

Divorced Mother's Travel with Child: Conditions and Limits
Ruling with proof, detailing conditions and scholarly difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a divorced mother may travel with her child and apply the Hanafi distinction between revocable and irrevocable divorce.

Core Idea
A divorced mother may only take her child to her original hometown where the marriage took place — both conditions required.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the detailed ruling on a divorced mother's right to travel with her child in custody. The matn lays out the basic rule: she may only travel to her hometown where she was married. The commentary expands on distance, type of divorce, and the difference of opinion on whether both conditions are needed. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between revocable and irrevocable divorce, and analysing the implied consent of the father.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is the divorced mother's travel with her child in custody. The default ruling is that it is not permissible (haram) unless specific conditions are met.
The burden of proof is on the mother to show the conditions are satisfied.
2
Analyse the distance conditions
The commentary divides travel by distance: short (father can visit and return in a day) vs long. For short distance, travel may be permissible if other conditions hold; for long distance, it is only permissible if the destination is the mother's hometown where marriage occurred.
Short distance is defined by custom, not a fixed number of miles.
3
Specify the type of divorce
The ruling applies only to irrevocable divorce (bain). For revocable divorce or widowhood during iddah, travel is not allowed without permission.
The term 'muttallaqa' in the matn is specified as bain by the commentary.
4
Examine the dual condition
The phrase 'to her hometown where he married her' implies two conditions: the destination must be her original hometown (watan), and the marriage contract must have taken place there. Both must be fulfilled.
An exception exists for Dar al-Harb under certain circumstances.
5
Evaluate the ikhtilaf
Some scholars suffice with the contract location alone, citing the hadith 'Whoever settles in a town, he is from its people.' The Hanafi relied-upon view requires both conditions because the contract in a foreign land does not imply permanent residence or the father's consent.
The hadith is ahad and zanni, so it can be overridden by istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تسافر مطلقةً بولدها إلا إلى وطنها الذي نكحها فيه
(1) قوله : ولا تسافر...الخ ؛ تفصيلُ هذه المسألة على ما في «الهداية» و«الكنز» وشروحهما : إنّه لا يجوز للمطلقة الخروج بولدها الذي هو في حضانتها من المصر إلى القرية، وإن كان بينهما تفاوت قليل ؛ لاحتمال ضرر الولد، بتخلقه بأخلاق أهل السوء، ومن المصر إلى المصر. أو من القرية إلى المصر يجوز إن كان بينهما تفاوت قليل، بحيث يمكن للزوج أن يبصر ولده ثم يرجع في يومه ؛ لأنه كانتقال من محلة مصر إلى محلة أخرى ، وإن كان بينهما تفاوت كثير، سواءً كان مدّة السفر أو ما دونه فلا يجوز له، إلا إذا كان ما انتقلت إليه وطنها، وقد نكحها زوجها: أي أبو ولدها هناك.
[٣] قوله : مطلقة ؛ أي بالطلاق البائن، فإنّ المطلقة الرجعية حكمها حكمُ المنكوحة، ليس لهما الخروج مطلقاً من بيت العدّة ، وكذا المعتدة مطلقاً ليس لها الخروج قبل انقضاء العدة. كذا في «البحر». وقال الخير الرملي : الظاهرُ أنّ المتوفى عنها زوجها كالمطلقة في ذلك، فلا تملك ذلك بلا إذن الأولياء ؛ لقيامهم مقام الأب وما فيه إضرار بالولد ظاهر المنع.
[٤] قوله : إلا إلى وطنها ؛ ظاهره أنه لا بُدَّ من تحقق الأمرين، وهو أن يكون المنتقل إليه وطناً لها، وأن يكون وقع عقدها بوالد ولدها فيه، سواء كان ذلك مصراً أو قرية، ويستثنى منه دار الحرب، فإنّه لو كان وطنها ووقع العقد فيه ثمّ أسلما وأقاما بدارِ الإسلام لا يحل لها الانتقال إلى دار الحرب إلا إذا كان الزوجان كافرين مستأمنين فلها ذلك. كذا في «البدائع». وذهب بعض المشايخ إلى أن يكفي محلّ الخروج كون المنتقل إليه مما وقع العقد فيه وإن لم يكن ذلك وطنها ؛ لحديث: «مَن تأهّل في بلدة فهو من أهلها، أخرجه أحمد وابن أبي شيبة وغيرهما. وذكر في «الهداية» وشروحها : إنّ الأصح هو اعتبار الأمرين ؛ لأنَّ العقد في دار الغربة لا يكون التزاماً للقيام فيها عرفاً، فلا يكفي مجرد وقوع العقد، وكذا لا يكفي مجرد كونه وطنها لها ؛ إذ قد يكون وقوع العقد في بلدة أخرى موجباً للإقامة هناك، وهجران الوطن الأصلي. والحاصل : أنه إذا كان المنتقل إليه وطنها وقد كان وقع العقد فيه يحلّ له الخروج بولدها إليه ؛ لتحقق رضاء أبيه به دلالة وإلا فلا يحلّ.
[١] قوله : وهذا ؛ يعني ما ذكر من الحكم مع التفصيل الذي مر إنّما هو في الأمّ المطلقة، وأما غيرها من الحاضنات كالجدة وغيرها فلا يجوز لهم الخروج بذلك الولد الذي في حضانتهم إلى أوطانهم أيضاً إلا بإذن والده ؛ لأنَّ العقد على الزوجة في وطنها دليل الرضا بإقامتها بالولد فيه، وهذا مفقود في حق غيرها من الحاضنات.
And a divorced woman should not travel with her child except to her hometown in which he married her. (1) He said: 'And she should not travel...' etc. The detail of this issue according to al-Hidayah, al-Kanz, and their commentaries: It is not permissible for the divorced woman to take out her child who is in her custody from a city to a village, even if the distance between them is little, because of potential harm to the child by adopting the morals of bad people. And from a city to another city, or from a village to a city, it is permissible if the distance between them is little, such that the husband can see his child and return within the same day, because it is like moving from one neighborhood of a city to another. If the distance is great, whether it is a travel distance or less, it is not permissible for her, unless the place she moves to is her hometown, and her husband (the father of her child) married her there. [3] He said: 'divorced': meaning by irrevocable divorce (bain). For a revocably divorced woman, her ruling is like that of a married woman; neither of them may leave the house of iddah absolutely. Likewise, any woman in iddah may not leave before the iddah expires. So in al-Bahr. Al-Khayr al-Ramli said: The apparent view is that a woman whose husband has died is like the divorced woman in this matter, so she may not do so without the permission of the guardians, because they stand in place of the father, and whatever harms the child is obviously prohibited. [4] He said: 'except to her hometown': Its apparent meaning is that both conditions must be fulfilled: that the destination is her hometown and that her marriage contract to the father of her child took place there, whether it is a city or a village. Dar al-Harb is excepted: if it was her hometown and the contract took place there, then they both embraced Islam and resided in Dar al-Islam, it is not permissible for her to move to Dar al-Harb unless both spouses were disbelievers and under safe conduct, then it is permissible for her. So in al-Bada'i'. Some scholars opined that it is sufficient that the place of departure or destination is where the contract took place, even if it is not her hometown, based on the hadith: 'Whoever settles in a town, he is from its people' narrated by Ahmad, Ibn Abi Shaybah, and others. It is mentioned in al-Hidayah and its commentaries that the more correct view is the consideration of both conditions, because the contract in a foreign land is not an undertaking to reside there customarily, so mere occurrence of the contract is not sufficient. Similarly, mere fact that it is her hometown is not sufficient, as the contract may have taken place in another city necessitating residence there and abandoning the original hometown. The bottom line: if the destination is her hometown and the contract took place there, she may take out her child to it because the father’s consent is indicated by implication; otherwise, it is not permissible. [1] He said 'This': meaning what was mentioned of the ruling with the detail that preceded is only for the divorced mother. As for other female custodians like the grandmother and others, they may not take out the child in their custody to their hometowns either without the permission of the father, because the contract with the wife in her hometown is evidence of consent to her residence with the child there, and this is absent in the case of other custodians.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed ruling on when a divorced mother may travel with her child. The matn states that she may only travel to her hometown where her marriage contract took place. The commentary expands on several sub-issues. First, it distinguishes between types of travel: moving from a city to a village is prohibited even if the distance is small due to potential harm to the child. Moving from one city to another or from village to city is permissible if the distance is short enough for the father to visit and return within the day. If the distance is great, it is not allowed unless the destination is her hometown where the marriage occurred. Second, 'divorced' refers only to an irrevocable divorce (bain); a revocably divorced woman is still considered married and cannot leave the iddah house. Similarly, a widow is like a divorced woman and needs permission from guardians. Third, the condition of 'her hometown' requires two elements: the destination must be her hometown, and the marriage contract must have taken place there. An exception is made for Dar al-Harb. There is a difference of opinion: some scholars suffice with the contract location based on a hadith, but the correct view as per al-Hidayah requires both conditions. Finally, this ruling applies exclusively to the mother, not to other female custodians like grandmothers, who cannot travel with the child without the father's permission because the implied consent from the marriage contract does not apply to them.

Key Terms
مطلقة بائن (muṭallaqah bāʾin): A woman divorced irrevocably (bain), after which the marriage is completely dissolved and she must observe iddah.
وطن (waṭan): Hometown or domicile; a place considered one's permanent residence.
حضانة (ḥaḍānah): Legal custody of a child, including physical care and upbringing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Permissible because the distance is short
B) Permissible if she has the father's permission
C) Not permissible
D) Not permissible unless she returns to her original hometown

Key Takeaway
A divorced mother may only take her child to her hometown where her marriage took place; both conditions must be met for the travel to be permissible.

Conditions for a Divorced Mother to Move with Her Child
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mother's relocation conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions required for a divorced mother to move with her child according to the relied-upon Hanafi view, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sufficiency of the marriage contract location alone.

Core Idea
A divorced mother may move with her child only to her hometown where the marriage occurred; both conditions are needed per the relied-upon view.

The Hanafi school holds that a divorced mother's right to relocate with her child is not absolute. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a key dispute: some Hanafi mashayikh argue that the marriage contract location alone suffices, citing a hadith, but the relied-upon position requires both the mother's original hometown (watan) and the contract location. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly applying custody rules in family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State the relied-upon (al-ashahh) Hanafi position: the mother may move only if the destination is her watan AND the marriage contract occurred there.
2
Recognize the alternative view
Some Hanafi mashayikh hold that only the contract location matters, even if it is not her watan.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence
The alternative view cites 'Man ta'ahhala fi baladatin fa huwa min ahliha'. Examine how this hadith is used as a proof.
The hadith is zanni in both transmission and meaning.
4
Follow the Hanafi rebuttal
The author argues that a contract in a foreign land (dar al-ghurba) does not imply commitment to reside there, and merely being her watan is insufficient if the contract occurred elsewhere. Hence both conditions are needed.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If only the contract occurred in a non-watan city, the mother cannot move there without father's permission per the relied-upon view, but some would permit it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
