(١) في «مشكل الآثار» (٩ : ٢١٤)، وغيره.
And this is only for the mother: meaning the aforementioned travel. A village, and dar al-harb is excluded from it; for if it was her home (watan) and the contract took place there, then they both embraced Islam and took residence in dar al-Islam, it is not permissible for her to move to dar al-harb unless both spouses are disbelieving musta'min, then she may do so. Thus in al-Bada'i'. Some mashayikh held that it suffices for the place of departure that the destination is where the contract took place, even if it is not her home, due to the hadith: 'Whoever marries in a town is considered one of its people,' reported by Ahmad, Ibn Abi Shaybah, and others. It is mentioned in al-Hidayah and its commentaries that the most correct view is to consider both matters; because the contract in a foreign land (dar al-ghurba) does not constitute a commitment to reside there customarily, so mere occurrence of the contract is insufficient. Similarly, merely it being her home is insufficient; since the contract taking place in another town may necessitate residence there and abandonment of the original home. In summary: if the destination is her home and the contract took place there, then it is permissible for her to move with her child to it, because the father's consent is established indicatively; otherwise it is not permissible. [1] He said: 'And this' means what was mentioned of the ruling with the aforementioned detail applies only to the divorced mother. As for other custodians such as the grandmother and others, they are not allowed to move with the child who is in their custody to their own homes except with the father's permission; because the marriage contract with the wife in her home is evidence of consent for her to reside there with the child, and this is absent in the case of other custodians.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling regarding a divorced mother's right to move with her child to another location. The text begins by stating that the previously mentioned ruling about travel applies only to the mother. It then discusses a key question: under what conditions may a divorced mother move with her child to a town? The relied-upon Hanafi position, as stated in al-Hidayah, requires two conditions: (1) the destination must be the mother's original home (watan), and (2) the marriage contract must have taken place there. Some Hanafi mashayikh argued that only the contract location matters, citing a hadith: 'Whoever marries in a town is considered one of its people.' However, al-Hidayah responds that a marriage contract in a foreign land does not imply a commitment to reside there, so it is insufficient alone. Similarly, merely being her home is insufficient because the contract might have occurred elsewhere, creating a different obligation. Therefore, both conditions are necessary. The text also emphasizes that this ruling applies only to the mother; other custodians (e.g., grandmother) cannot move with the child without the father's permission, since the father's implied consent is based on the marriage contract in the mother's home.

Key Terms
وطن (watan): The original home or permanent residence of the mother, where she is considered a resident.
حاضن (hadin): A person who has custody (hadanah) of a child; in this context, the mother or other female relatives like the grandmother.
دار الغربة (dar al-ghurbah): A foreign land or a place that is not one's original home; where one is a stranger.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The city must be her original home (watan) and the marriage contract must have taken place there.
B) The city must be her original home (watan) only.
C) The marriage contract must have taken place there only.
D) The father must give explicit permission regardless of conditions.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position requires both the mother's watan and the contract location for her to move with her child; otherwise, she needs the father's permission.

The Standard for Determining Maintenance Amount in Hanafi Law
Explaining the preferred Hanafi position on maintenance standard as an intra-madhhab tarjih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Zahir al-Riwaya position from the mufta bihi position on maintenance standard, and apply the ruling to cases where spouses differ in financial status.

Core Idea
The mufta bihi in Hanafi maintenance considers both spouses' states, not just the husband's.

This passage clarifies an important nuance: the original Hanafi position (Zahir al-Riwaya) considered only the husband's state for maintenance amount, similar to the Shafi'i view. However, the later relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) takes a different path, combining the husband's and wife's states to determine a moderate amount. This shift, championed by al-Khassaf and adopted by the majority of later mashayikh, shows how the school evolved its practical rulings while remaining faithful to the textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Zahir al-Riwaya position
Identify the original transmitted ruling from Abu Hanifa: consider only the husband's state.
This is also the Shafi'i position.
2
Identify the opposing intra-Hanafi view
Who proposed considering both states? Al-Khassaf and the majority of later scholars.
This view became the mufta bihi.
3
Follow the evidence for each view
For husband's state: Quran 65:7 (apparent meaning) and principle of capacity. For both states: same verse plus hadith of Umm Salama.
The hadith shows the wife's criterion.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How do those who consider both states reconcile the two proofs? By combining the verse (husband's criterion) and hadith (wife's criterion), which necessitates considering both.
This is a classic tawfiq (reconciliation) of proofs.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and authority shift
Which view is relied upon for fatwa? Al-Khassaf's view, adopted by the majority of later scholars, is the operative ruling.
This shows that the mufta bihi can differ from the original Zahir al-Riwaya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو
هذا عندنا (١) ، وأما عند الشَّافِعِي(٢) له فالمعتبر حال الزوج.
[١] قوله : هذا عندنا ؛ أي اعتبار حالي الزوجين واعتبار الوسط عند اختلاف حالهما مذهبنا، وهذا يوهمُ أنّه متفق عليه بين أصحابنا، أو أنه أصل مذهب أصحابنا مطلقاً، وليس كذلك ؛ فإنه مذهب اختاره الخصاف له وأفتى به جمهور المشايخ. وأما أصلُ مذهب أصحابنا المذكور في ظاهر الراوية، فهو اعتبار حال الرجل مثل مذهب الشافعي له ، ثم الحجّة لمن اعتبر حال الزوج مطلقاً ظاهر قوله تعالى: {لِيُنفِقْ ذُو سَعَةٍ مِّن سَعَتِهِ} (٣) الآية. ويؤيده أن الرجل لا يكلّف إلا بما في وسعه، فتكون النفقة واجبة عليه على حاله ، وهي إن كانت غنيّة فلما تزوّجت بمعسر فقد رضيت بنفقة الإعسار، فلا تستوجب على الزوج زيادة.
ومن حكم باعتبار حالهما احتج بالآية المذكورة، وبحديث زوجة أبي سفيان الذي ذكرناه سابقاً ؛ فإنّ الآية تدلّ على اعتبار حال الزوج ، وقوله : «خذي ما يكفيك وولدك بالمعروف يدلّ على اعتبار حالها، فوجب الجمع بينهما، ولا يكون ذلك إلا باعتبار حالهما ، وزيادة التفصيل في هذا البحث في شروح «الهداية».
(١) اتفقوا على وجوب نفقة الموسرين إذا كانا موسرين وعلى نفقة المعسرين إذا كانا معسرين، واختلفوا إذا كان أحدهما معسرا والآخر موسرا على قولين :
الأول : بقدر حالهما : وهو قول الخصاف ، وبه يفتى كما في «الهداية»(٢ : ٣٩)، ودرر الحكام (١ : ٤١٣)، وشرح ملا مسكين (ص ١٣٢)، وفتح باب العناية» (٢: ١٩٢)، و(الدر المختار (١ : ٦٤٥)، واختاره المصنف والشارح وصاحب «الكتاب»(ص ٨٢)، و«الكنز» (ص ٦٥) ، و(الملتقى» (ص ٧٣)، وغيرهم.
والثاني : يعتبر حاله : وهو قول الكرخي له ، وظاهر الرواية ، وفي (التحفة»(٢ : ١٦٠)، و(البدائع (٤ : ٢٤): وهو الصحيح. (٢) ينظر: ((التنبيه (ص ١٢٩) ، و(المنهاج» وشرحه (مغني المحتاج» (٣: ٤٢٦)، وغيرها. (٣) الطلاق : من الآية .
And if... This is our position (1). As for al-Shafi'i (2), the consideration is the husband's state. (And if she...) [1] His statement: 'this is our position' means considering the state of both spouses and taking the average when they differ is our madhhab. This gives the impression that it is agreed upon among our companions, or that it is the original position of our companions absolutely, but it is not so. Rather, it is a position chosen by al-Khassaf and given fatwa by the majority of the mashayikh. As for the original madhhab of our companions mentioned in Zahir al-Riwaya, it is considering the state of the man, like the madhhab of al-Shafi'i. Then the proof for those who consider the husband's state absolutely is the apparent meaning of His statement: 'Let the wealthy man spend according to his means' (3) (65:7). And it is supported by the fact that a man is only obligated according to his capacity, so maintenance is obligatory on him according to his state. And if she is wealthy, when she married a poor man, she consented to the maintenance of poverty, so she does not deserve an increase from the husband. Those who ruled by considering both states argued with the aforementioned verse and the hadith of the wife of Abu Sufyan which we mentioned earlier. Indeed, the verse indicates considering the husband's state, and his statement: 'Take what suffices you and your child in a reasonable manner' indicates considering her state, so it is necessary to combine them, and that is only possible by considering both states. Further detail on this research is in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. (1) They agreed on the obligation of maintenance for the rich if both are rich, and on the maintenance of the poor if both are poor, and they differed when one is poor and the other is rich on two opinions: The first: according to the state of both: that is the saying of al-Khassaf, and fatwa is given by it as in al-Hidayah (2:39), Durar al-Hukkam (1:413), Sharh Mulla Miskin (p.132), Fath Bab al-'Inaya (2:192), al-Durr al-Mukhtar (1:645), and it was chosen by the author, the commentator, the author of al-Kitab (p.82), al-Kanz (p.65), al-Multaqa (p.73), and others. The second: the husband's state is considered: that is the saying of al-Karkhi, and it is the apparent riwaya, and in al-Tuhfa (2:160) and al-Bada'i' (4:24): it is the correct one. (2) See: al-Tanbih (p.129), al-Minhaj and its Sharh Mughni al-Muhtaj (3:426), and others. (3) At-Talaq: from the verse.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the standard for determining the amount of maintenance (nafaqa). The matn (from al-Wiqaya) states the preferred Hanafi position: consider both spouses' states and take an average. The commentator clarifies that this is not the original position of the school; rather, the original position in Zahir al-Riwaya (the relied-upon six books of Imam Muhammad) is to consider only the husband's state, akin to the Shafi'i view. The author then attributes the choice of considering both states to al-Khassaf, who is followed by the majority of later mashayikh in fatwa. The passage provides the scriptural evidence for each view. For considering the husband alone: the Quranic verse (65:7) and the principle that the husband is only obligated within his capacity. For considering both: the same verse (which shows the husband's criterion) combined with the hadith of Umm Salama (the wife of Abu Sufyan) where the Prophet ﷺ told her to take what suffices her and her child, which shows the wife's criterion. The commentator then notes that the original Zahir al-Riwaya position (husband's state) is still considered correct by some, but the fatwa is on al-Khassaf's position.

Key Terms
اعتبار حال الزوجين (i'tibar hal al-zawjayn): Considering the financial states of both spouses to determine a moderate maintenance amount.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted position from Abu Hanifa and his students in the six authoritative books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Based solely on the husband's financial state.
B) Based solely on the wife's financial state.
C) Based on both spouses' financial states, taking an average.
D) Based on the financial state of the husband's family.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position for maintenance amount considers both spouses' states, not only the husband's.

Dropping of Determined Maintenance upon Death or Divorce
States Hanafi ruling and Shafi'i disagreement on determined maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on determined maintenance dropping upon death or divorce and explain the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Determined maintenance drops at death or divorce because it is a gift, not a debt.

When a husband and wife agree on a specific amount of maintenance for a past period, that amount is due only while both are alive. If death or divorce occurs before payment, the obligation drops in the Hanafi school—unless the wife borrowed by court order. The Shafi'i school treats it as a debt. This lesson unpacks the proofs and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine the exact legal question: what happens to agreed maintenance (mafrud) for a past period if one spouse dies or divorce occurs before the wife receives it?
The dispute centers on whether it drops or becomes a debt.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the obligation drops entirely, unless she borrowed by court order. Note the intra-Hanafi caution regarding revocable divorce (raj‘i) to prevent trickery.
The explicit exception: borrowing by court order preserves the debt.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Maintenance is a gift (sila) for the wife's being withheld (ihtibas). Gifts are not owned until possession; if possession is prevented by death or divorce, the obligation lapses.
Compare with dowry (mahr): dowry is compensation, so it becomes a debt.
4
Consider the opposing view
Al-Shafi‘i argues that determined maintenance becomes a debt like the dowry, surviving death or divorce. Identify his proof and the Hanafi rebuttal.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi wife cannot claim; Shafi‘i wife can.
5
Apply the practical consequence
In a scenario: husband dies before paying. Under Hanafi, the wife gets nothing; under Shafi‘i, the estate must pay. Recognize which is mufta bihi.
The fatwa reliance is on the Hanafi position, with caution in raj‘i divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو رضيا بشيء، فتجب لما مَضَى ما داما حيين فإن مات أحدهما أو طلقها قبل قبض سَقَط المفروض إلا إذا استدانت بأمر قاض(۱) : هذا عندنا، وأما عند الشَّافِعِي، فلا تسقط بالموت، بل تصير ديناً عليه. [1] قوله : أو طلقها...الخ ؛ طلاقاً بائناً كان أو رجعياً، وصحح في «خزانة المفتين»: إنّه لا يفتي بالسقوط في الرجعي ؛ لئلا يتخذه الناس حيلة. [٢] قوله : بل تصير ديناً عليه ؛ كسائر الديون فيلزم على الزوج أداؤها إلى ورثة الزوجة إن ماتت وعلى ورثته أداؤها إلى الزوجة إن مات، وهذا كالمهر لا يسقط بالموت ولا بالطلاق. ونحن نقول : إن المهرّ عوض عن منافع البضع، والنفقة صلة جزاء للاحتباس، والصلات لا تملك قبل القبض، وتسقط بموت أحدهما.
Or if they both agree on something, then it becomes due for what has passed as long as both are alive. Then if one of them dies or he divorces her before receiving, the determined [maintenance] drops, unless she borrowed by order of a judge. This is according to us. As for al-Shafi'i, it does not drop by death, but rather becomes a debt upon him. [1] He said: 'or he divorces her... etc.' — whether it is a final (ba'in) or revocable (raj'i) divorce, and it is corrected in Khizanat al-Muftin that one should not give a fatwa of dropping in the revocable case, so that people do not use it as a trick. [2] He said: 'but rather becomes a debt upon him' — like other debts, it becomes obligatory for the husband to pay it to the wife's heirs if she dies, and for his heirs to pay it to the wife if he dies. This is like the dowry, which does not drop by death or divorce. And we say: Indeed the dowry is compensation for the benefits of the vulva, while maintenance is a gift as a reward for being withheld, and gifts are not owned before possession, and they drop with the death of one of them.
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning determined maintenance (mafrud). The Hanafi position is that if a husband and wife agree on a specific amount of maintenance for a past period while they are still married, it becomes due. However, if one of them dies or the husband divorces her before she receives it, the obligation to pay that amount drops entirely, unless she had borrowed money for her upkeep by order of a judge. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that once the maintenance is determined, it becomes a debt that survives death and divorce, similar to the dowry. The Hanafi commentator explains that a revocable divorce is included in the ruling, but a fatwa caution is added to avoid trickery. The Hanafi refutation is that maintenance is a gift (sila) for the wife's being available (ihtibas), and gifts are only owned upon possession; if not yet possessed, they drop at death. The dowry, by contrast, is a compensation for sexual access and thus becomes a debt. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that under the Hanafi school, the wife or her heirs cannot claim that sum after the death of either spouse, whereas the Shafi'i school treats it as a recoverable debt.

Key Terms
المفروض (al-mafrud): The determined amount of maintenance agreed upon by the spouses or set by a judge.
صلة (silah): A gift or favor given as a reward for being withheld (ihtibas).
الاحتباس (al-ihtibas): The wife's being available and confined to the husband for marital relations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband agrees to pay past maintenance but dies before paying, what happens?
A) The obligation becomes a debt on his estate.
B) The obligation drops unless she borrowed by court order.
C) The wife receives half the amount.
D) The obligation is transferred to the husband's heirs as a gift.

Key Takeaway
Determined maintenance drops upon death or divorce because it is a gift (sila) for ihtibas, not a debt like mahr.

The Nature of a Slave's Maintenance: Gift or Compensation?
Commentary on the ruling for a slave's wife's maintenance and the sale of the slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the reclamation of prepaid maintenance (nafaqah) for a slave's wife, explain the legal reasoning (silah vs. iwad), and apply the conditions for selling the slave to cover the debt.

Core Idea
Prepaid maintenance is a gift; once received, it is not reclaimable upon death, unlike ordinary compensation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses a crucial question: if a slave's wife receives advance maintenance and then one spouse dies, can the prepaid amount be reclaimed? The Hanafi school's answer hinges on whether maintenance is classified as a gift (silah) or as compensation (iwad). The decision has practical implications for the wife's estate and the slave's employer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute is whether prepaid nafaqah can be reclaimed after the death of either spouse before the period expires.
2
Understand the Hanafi position
Maintenance is a silah (gift) in nature, so it becomes owned upon receipt and is not reclaimable upon death.
Even small amounts (a month or less) are not reclaimed.
3
Examine the proof
Analogy to hibah: once a gift is taken possession of, it is irrevocable upon death. This is the primary dalil.
The analogy is strengthened by consensus: if the prepaid sum perishes without consumption, nothing is reclaimed.
4
Compare with the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i argues it is compensation (iwad) for ihtibas, so it should be returned on death.
The Hanafi response: maintenance is not like other compensations, as shown by the consensus on destruction without consumption.
5
Apply the conditions for selling the slave
When maintenance is due and the slave cannot pay, he is sold, but only under specific conditions: marriage with permission, judge's prescription, and no ransom by the master.
The buyer's knowledge of the debt affects his right to return the slave for defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق/باب النفقة
ونفقة عرس القن
[1] قوله : لأنها صلة...الخ ؛ حاصله : أنّ النفقة صلةٌ في نفسها وإن وجبت جزاءً للاحتباس ، والصلاتُ تملك بعد القبض، ويسقط حق الرجوع فيها عند الموت. [2] قوله : كما في الهبة ؛ فإنّه إذا ملك الموهوب له الموهوب بالقبض ، ثم مات الواهب أو الموهوب له لا يجوز رجوع الواهب أو ورثته فيها. [3] قوله : وعند محمد الله ؛ هذا رواية ابن رستم عنه ، وفي رواية عنه : إنها إذا قبضت نفقة شهر فما دونها لا تسترد منها شيء؛ لأنه يسير، فصار في حكم الحال وما زاد عليه تسترد. [4] قوله : لأنها عوض.... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنّ النفقة إنمـا تجـب لـهـا عـلـيـه بـسبب الاحتباس فهي عوض عنه ، فإذا مات أحدهما قبل مضي تلك المدة التي عجلت ليها نفقتها بطل الاستحقاق لفوات الاحتباس بالموت، فيسترد العوض بعينه إن كان قائما ، وقيمته إن كان مستهلكاً كما في سائر الأعواض. والجواب عنه من قبلهما : إن وجوب النفقة وإن كان بالاحتباس لكنها ليست كسائر الأعواض ؛ ولهذا لو هلكت نفقة معجلة مدة من غير استهلاك، ثمّ مات أحدهما لا يُستَرَدُّ شيء منها بالإجماع.
[0] قوله : عرس القِن ؛ العِرس - بالكسر - : الزوجة كما مرّ، والقن - بكسر القاف وتشديد النون - عند الفقهاء عبارة عمّن لا حريّة فيه بوجه من الوجوه، وأشار به إلى أنه لا يُباع غير القن من المدبّر والمكاتب ؛ لعدم صحة بيعهما، بل يلزم عليها السعاية ؛ لأداء النفقة. كذا في «البحر».

[1] قوله : عليه ؛ أي واجبة ولازمة على القِنّ لا على مولاه، وهذا إذا كانت الزوجة حرّة ، فإنّه تجب عليها نفقتها ، وأما إذا كانت زوجة القنّ أمة ، فإن كانت أمة لمولاه فلا تجب نفقتها على الزوج ؛ لأنهما جميعاً ملك المولى، ونفقة المملوك على المالك. وأما إن كانت أمةً لغيره فلا تجب عليه نفقتها إلا إذا بوأها مولاها – أي دفعها إلى الزوج - ، ولم يستخدمها وإلا فلا. كذا في «البحر» وغيره. 
[2] قوله : يُباع ؛ أي يبيعه سيّده ، ويؤدّي من ثمنه النفقة ؛ لأنها دين تعلّق برقبته، فيؤمر ببيعه فإن امتنع عنه باعه القاضي، وهذا مشروط بشروط : أحدهما : أن يكون نكاح العبد بإذن السيد، فإنّه إذا تزوج الفن أو المدبر ونحوهما بلا إذن مولاه فلا نفقة عليه ولا مهر ؛ لعدم صحة النكاح، فإذا أعتق أحدهم جازَ نکاحه حين عتق ، فيجب عليه المهرُ والنفقة في المستقبل، ويطالب هو بهما. وبالجملة : لا بيع في هذه الصورة. وثانيها : أن تكون النفقةُ مفروضة فرضها القاضي ؛ لأنها بدون الفرض تسقط بالمضي: كنفقة زوجة الحرّ، وقد أشار الشارح له إلى هذين الشرطين في تصوير المسألة. وثالثها: أن لا يختار المولى فداءه، فإن اختار المولى ذلك فلا يُباع ؛ لأن حقها إنما هو في النفقة لا في الرقبة. كذا في «النهر»، و«الفتح».

Book of Divorce / Chapter of Maintenance. And the maintenance of the wife of the slave. [1] His saying: Because it is a gift...etc.; its summary: that maintenance is a gift in itself even if it is obligatory as a compensation for retention, and gifts are owned after possession, and the right of return is dropped upon death. [2] His saying: As in a gift; for if the donee owns the gifted item by possession, then the donor or donee dies, the donor or his heirs may not revoke it. [3] His saying: According to Muhammad; this is a narration from Ibn Rustam from him, and in another narration from him: if she receives maintenance for a month or less, nothing is reclaimed from her because it is small, so it is like a present, and anything more is reclaimed. [4] His saying: Because it is a compensation... etc.; its summary: that maintenance is only obligatory on him due to retention, so it is a compensation for it. If one of them dies before the expiry of that period for which her maintenance was given in advance, the entitlement becomes void due to loss of retention by death, so the compensation is reclaimed in kind if it still exists, or its value if it is consumed as in other compensations. The answer from them [Imam Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] is: although the obligation of maintenance is due to retention, it is not like other compensations; hence if prepaid maintenance for a period is destroyed without consumption, then one of them dies, nothing is reclaimed from it by consensus. [0] His saying: The wife of the slave; al-irs (with kasr) means wife as before, and al-qinn (with kasr of qaf and shadda on nun) according to jurists is an expression for one who has no freedom in any way. He indicated by this that besides the qinn, the mudabbar and mukatab are not sold because their sale is not valid; rather, [the slave] is required to earn to pay maintenance. Thus in al-Bahr. Upon him, he is sold for it repeatedly. [1] His saying: Upon him; i.e., it is obligatory and binding on the slave, not on his master, and this is when the wife is free; then her maintenance is obligatory on him. But if the slave's wife is a slave-girl, if she is a slave-girl of his master, her maintenance is not obligatory on the husband because both are owned by the master, and the maintenance of a slave is on the owner. And if she is a slave-girl of another, her maintenance is not obligatory on him unless her master gives her to him (i.e., hands her over to the husband) and does not employ her; otherwise not. Thus in al-Bahr and others. [2] His saying: He is sold; i.e., his master sells him and pays the maintenance from its price because it is a debt attached to his person; so he is commanded to sell him, and if he refuses, the judge sells him. This is conditioned by three conditions: First, that the slave's marriage be with the master's permission; if the qinn or mudabbar or the like marries without his master's permission, then no maintenance or mahr is due due to the invalidity of the marriage; if one of them is emancipated, his marriage becomes valid at the time of emancipation, so mahr and maintenance become obligatory in the future, and he is demanded for them. In short, there is no sale in this case. Second, that the maintenance be prescribed by the judge's ruling; because without prescription it is dropped by passage of time, like the maintenance of a free husband's wife. The commentator indicated these two conditions in illustrating the issue. Third, that the master does not choose to ransom him; if the master chooses that, he is not sold because her right is in maintenance, not in the slave's person. Thus in al-Nahr and al-Fath. [3] His saying: repeatedly; meaning if a prescribed maintenance accumulates on him, he is sold for it; then if another maintenance accumulates, he is sold again, and so on. This is if the buyer knows that he has a debt of maintenance, or does not know then knows and is satisfied with it. But if he does not know his situation, or knows after purchase and is not satisfied, he has the right to return him because it is a defect he discovered. He is only sold repeatedly because maintenance is owed little by little; so what is owed again is a new debt incurred by him, so he is sold for it, and so on. Thus in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding the maintenance (nafaqah) of a slave's wife. The author begins by discussing the nature of maintenance: is it a gift (silah) or compensation (iwad)? The commentator explains that maintenance is a gift in essence even though it is obligatory as compensation for the wife's retention (ihtibas). Therefore, like a gift, once the wife receives it, she owns it, and if either spouse dies, the maintenance is not reclaimable. This is contrasted with the view of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani in one narration (from Ibn Rustam), who distinguishes between small amounts (a month or less) which are not reclaimed, and larger amounts which are reclaimed. The Shafi'i school holds that prepaid maintenance is compensation and thus reclaimable upon death. The Hanafi response is that maintenance is not like other compensations; if the money perishes without consumption, nothing is reclaimed by consensus. 

Then the commentator explains the term 'urs al-qinn (wife of the slave). Al-qinn is a slave with no freedom. The ruling applies only to the qinn, not to the mudabbar or mukatab, whose sale is invalid; instead, such slaves must work to pay their wife's maintenance. 

The passage then details that the slave is sold to cover his wife's maintenance, as it is a debt attached to his person. The sale occurs repeatedly if multiple periods of maintenance accumulate. Conditions for the sale: (1) the marriage must have been with the master's permission; otherwise the marriage is invalid and no maintenance is due. (2) The maintenance must have been prescribed by a judge; otherwise it lapses with time. (3) The master may choose to ransom the slave instead of allowing the sale, because the wife's right is in the maintenance, not in the slave's person. The buyer's knowledge of the debt affects his right to return the slave for defect.

Key Terms
عرس (urs): Wife; specifically, the wife of a slave.
قن (qinn): A slave who has no freedom whatsoever (a full slave).
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance; financial support owed to a wife including food, clothing, and shelter.
احتباس (ihtibas): The wife's retention of herself in the husband's home; the legal cause for maintenance.
صلة (silah): A gift or beneficial grant; something given without expectation of return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, if a husband prepays a year's maintenance to his wife and she dies after one month, what happens to the remaining 11 months' maintenance?
A) The heirs must return the remaining amount.
B) The remaining amount is not reclaimed and belongs to the wife's estate.
C) The remaining amount is divided equally between the heirs and the husband.
D) The husband can demand the remaining amount only if it is still physically present.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, prepaid maintenance is treated as a gift (silah) owned upon receipt, making it non-reclaimable upon death, unlike the Shafi'i view that treats it as compensation (iwad).

Housing Requirements for a Divorced Wife
Ruling with proof and conditions for the divorced wife's housing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for providing housing to a divorced wife and explain their basis from the Quran.

Core Idea
A divorced wife must be housed separately from the husband's family, with a lockable room, unless she consents otherwise.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah details the obligation of providing housing (sukna) to a divorced wife during her iddah. It is based on the Quranic command in Surah al-Talaq (65:6) and elaborates on the required conditions: the dwelling must be free of the husband's family, lockable, and separate. The commentary also clarifies key terms like bayt, manzil, and dar, and addresses exceptions. Let's unpack the ruling step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation and its proof
The passage begins by stating that housing is obligatory (wajib). It quotes Quran 65:6 as the primary dalil, interpreting the imperative 'askunuhunna' as an obligation akin to maintenance.
The Hanafi principle that al-amr yufidu al-wujub is applied here.
2
Extract the condition: no family members in the house
The matn requires that no one from the husband's family reside in the same house, citing the Quranic prohibition against harming her (65:6 continued). The commentary expands that this includes close relatives, even the mother of his child, but excludes his slave woman due to need.
Children who do not understand intercourse are exempted.
3
Note the exception: consent of the wife
If the wife consents to living with the husband's family, the condition is waived because she has agreed to a reduction of her right.
Consent must be genuine and voluntary.
4
Understand the requirement of a separate room with a lock
The husband must provide a separate room (bayt) within the larger dwelling, with its own lock (ghalaq), ensuring privacy and preventing harm. The commentary from al-Bazzaziyya and al-Bada'i clarifies that a single room suffices if it meets these criteria.
The kitchen and latrine should also be private or at least not shared with others.
5
Define the dwelling terminology
The passage distinguishes between bayt (single roofed room with vestibule), manzil (rooms plus kitchen and toilet, no courtyard), and dar (compound with courtyard, rooms, veranda, etc.). The obligation can be fulfilled by any of these, provided the conditions are met.
The higher-level dar is not required; a bayt with lock is sufficient.

Source Text

ويجب سكناها في بيت ليس فيه أحد من أهله، ولو ولده من غيرها إلا برضاها، وبيت مفرد من دار له غلق. [١] قوله : ويجب؛ الأصل في وجوب المسكن قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم في سورة الطلاق: أَسْكُنُوهُنَّ مِنْ حَيْثُ سَكَنتُم مِّن وُجدكُمْ : أي طاقتكم، فيكون وجوب السكني كوجوب النفقة حالهما يساراً وإعساراً. [٢] قوله : في بيت؛ سواء كان ذلك ملكاً له أو إجارة أو عارية. [٣] قوله : ليس فيه؛ الجملة صفة لبيت، والأصل فيه قوله ما بعد آية السكني : وَلَا تُضَارُوهُنَّ لِتُضَيْقُوا عَلَيْهِنَّ، فإنّه نهى عن إضرار الزوجة، والبيت إذا كان فيها أحد من أهله تتضرر به من حيث أنها لا تقدر على الانبساط التام والمعاشرة والاستمتاع مع الزوج كلّما أرادت، ولا تأمن على متاعها وغيره. [٤] قوله : أحد من أهله؛ أي من أهل الزوج، يعني أعزّته ومتعلّقيه حتى أمّ الولد، لما أنّ معيّتها مورثة إلى الوحشة والمضرّة، وأما الأمة؛ أي أمة الزوج فله إسكانها معها؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى استخدامها كل حين. كذا في «المحيط» و«الفتح». [٥] قوله : ولو الواو وصليّة؛ أي ولو كان ولد الزوج من زوجة أخرى؛ ويستثنى منه طفله الذي لا يفهم الجماع، وما يتعلق به، فلا ضير في معيته. [٦] قوله : ولده من غيرها إلا برضاها؛ استثناء من قوله : «ليس فيه أحد.... الخ، يعني إن رضيت بقيام أهله فيه فهو جائز؛ لأنها رضيت بنقص حقها. [٧] قوله : وبيت مفرد... الخ؛ حاصله أنه يكفي لها بيت واحدٌ من دار مشتمل على بيوت كثيرة، بشرط أن يكون مفرداً عن البيوت الآخر؛ أي منحازا ومنفصلا عنها، بحيث لا تتضرر بمرور سكّان البيوت الأخر عليها، وتقتدر على حفظ متاعها، وقضاء حوائجها. قال في «البزازية»: أبت أن تسكن مع أحماء الزوج وفي الدارِ بيوت إن فرّغ لها بيتاً له غلق على حدة، وليس فيه أحدٌ منهم لا تتمكن من مطالبته ببيت آخر. انتهى. وفي «البدائع»: لو أراد أن يسكنها مع ضرتها أو مع أحمائها كأمه وأخته وبنته، فأبت فعليه أن يسكنها في منزل منفرد؛ لأنّ إباءها دليلُ الأذى والضرر؛ ولأنه يحتاج إلى جماعها ومعاشرتها في أي وقت يتفق، ولا يمكن ذلك مع ثالث، حتى لو كان في الدارِ بيوت وجعل لبيتها غلقا على حدة، قالوا ليس لها أن تطالبه بآخر. انتهى. واعلم أن هاهنا ثلاثة ألفاظ مستعملة في عباراتهم بيت، ومنزل، ودار، أعلاها آخرها، وأدناها أولها، وأوسطها أوسطها، فقد نقل والدي العلام أدخله الله دار السلام في «شرح الهداية» المسمّى «السقاية لعطشان الهداية»: عن «المجتبى»: إنّ الدار اسم لما يشتمل على الصحن والبيوت والصفّة والمطبخ والاصطبل، والمنزل : ما يشتمل على بيوت ومطبخ ومستراح دون الصحن، والبيت : اسم لمسقف واحد له دهليز انتهى. [٨] قوله : له غلق؛ الجملة صفة لبيت، كما أنّ قوله : «مفرد» صفة، والغلق بفتحتين : ما يغلق ويفتح بالمفتاح، ويقال له : القفل، وذكر في «الفتح» و«البحر» : إنّه ينبغي أن يكون المطبخ والكنيف داخل البيت أو في الدار، ولا يشاركها أحد فيهما من أهل الدار؛ لأن المقصود لا يحصل إلا به.
And it is obligatory to house her in a house in which there is none of his family, even if his child from another wife except with her consent, and a separate house from a dwelling having a lock. [1] His saying: And it is obligatory; the basis for the obligation of housing is His saying, may God bless him and grant him peace, in Surah al-Talaq: 'House them where you dwell according to your means': i.e., your capacity. So the obligation of housing is like the obligation of maintenance in both cases of ease and hardship. [2] His saying: in a house; whether that is property owned by him or leased or borrowed. [3] His saying: in which there is none; the clause is an adjective for house, and the basis for it is His saying after the verse of housing: 'And do not harm them to straiten them', for He prohibited harming the wife, and if the house has any of his family in it, she is harmed by it in that she is not able to be completely at ease and to socialize and enjoy intimacy with her husband whenever she wants, and she is not safe for her belongings and other things. [4] His saying: any of his family; meaning from the husband's family, i.e., his close relatives and dependents, even the mother of his child, because her company leads to loneliness and harm. As for the slave woman, i.e., the husband's slave woman, he may house her with her because he needs to employ her at all times. Thus in al-Muhit and al-Fath. [5] His saying: even if the waw is conjunctive; meaning even if it is the husband's child from another wife; and there is excepted from it his child who does not understand intercourse and what pertains to it, so there is no harm in his company. [6] His saying: his child from another wife except with her consent; an exception from his saying: 'in which there is none...' etc., meaning if she agrees to the presence of his family in it, it is permissible, because she consented to the diminution of her right. [7] His saying: and a separate house ... etc.; its summary is that one house from a dwelling containing many houses is sufficient for her, on condition that it is separate from the other houses, i.e., isolated and detached from them, such that she is not harmed by the passage of the residents of the other houses upon her, and she is able to preserve her belongings and fulfill her needs. It is stated in al-Bazzaziyya: If she refuses to live with the husband's in-laws and in the house there are rooms, if he vacates for her a room having a lock separately and there is none of them in it, she is not able to demand another house from him. End quote. And in al-Bada'i: If he wants to house her with her co-wife or with his in-laws like his mother, sister, and daughter, and she refuses, then he must house her in a separate residence, because her refusal is evidence of harm and injury, and because he needs to have intercourse with her and socialize at any time that occurs, and that is not possible with a third person. Even if in the house there are rooms and he makes for her room a lock separately, they said she does not have the right to demand another. End quote. And know that here there are three terms used in their expressions: bayt, manzil, and dar. The highest is the last, the lowest is the first, and the middle is the middle. My father, the great scholar (may God admit him to the abode of peace) transmitted in Sharh al-Hidayah called al-Siqaya li-Atashan al-Hidayah from al-Mujtaba: Indeed, dar is a name for what includes the courtyard, rooms, veranda, kitchen, and stable. Manzil is what includes rooms, kitchen, and toilet without the courtyard. Bayt is a name for a single roofed structure having a vestibule. End quote. [8] His saying: having a lock; the clause is an adjective for bayt, just as his saying 'separate' is an adjective. And al-ghalq (with two fatha) is what is locked and opened with a key, and it is called al-qufl. And it is mentioned in al-Fath and al-Bahr that the kitchen and latrine should be inside the house or in the dwelling, and none of the residents of the dwelling should share them with her, because the intended purpose is not achieved except with that.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling on the housing obligation for a divorced wife. It begins with the matn stating that the wife must be housed in a house where none of the husband's family reside, even his child from another wife unless she consents, and it must be a separate house with a lock. The commentary provides the Quranic proof (65:6) for the obligation of housing, establishing it as similar to maintenance. It then explains each condition: the house can be owned, rented, or borrowed; the prohibition of having family members is derived from the verse against harming her; 'family' includes all close relatives and even the umm walad, but excluding the husband's slave woman due to need. Children who do not understand intercourse are allowed. If she consents, the restriction is waived. The commentary clarifies that a separate room within a larger house is sufficient if it has its own lock and is isolated from other residents to avoid harm. It also cites authorities like al-Bazzaziyya and al-Bada'i for specific scenarios. Finally, it defines the three terms for dwelling (bayt, manzil, dar) to clarify the legal requirements.

Key Terms
سكنى (sukna): Housing, the obligation to provide a dwelling for the divorced wife during her iddah.
غلق (ghalaq): A lock, meaning a device to secure the door with a key; required for the wife's privacy.
بيت (bayt): A single roofed structure with a vestibule; the smallest unit of dwelling.
منزل (manzil): A dwelling that includes rooms, kitchen, and toilet but not a courtyard.
دار (dar): A compound that includes a courtyard, rooms, veranda, kitchen, and stable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimum dwelling that fulfills the housing obligation for a divorced wife?
A) An entire dar (compound)
B) A separate manzil (apartment with kitchen and toilet)
C) A separate bayt (room) with a lock, within a larger house
D) Any room in the husband's house, even if shared

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires the divorced wife to be housed in a separate, lockable room free from the husband's family, ensuring her privacy and dignity during iddah.

Conditions for Granting Maintenance from an Absent Husband's Property
Conditions for ruling maintenance from absent's property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a Hanafi judge may prescribe maintenance from the absent's property and explain why evidence alone is insufficient.

Core Idea
Maintenance from an absent's property requires acknowledgment or judicial knowledge of both property and relationship, not just evidence.

In this lesson, we examine the specific conditions under which a judge can order maintenance from the property of an absent husband. The Hanafi school requires either the custodian's acknowledgment or the judge's knowledge of both the property and the marital relationship. Without these, no ruling can be made due to the absence of a contesting party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the required acknowledgments
The absent person has property with a custodian (depositary, mudarib, or debtor). The claimant (wife, parent, or child) seeks maintenance. The custodian must acknowledge both that the property is with him and the relationship that entitles maintenance.
2
Alternative to acknowledgment: judicial knowledge
If the judge personally knows both facts (property and relationship), that suffices even without the custodian's acknowledgment.
3
Partial knowledge scenario
If the judge knows only one fact, the custodian must acknowledge the other. If neither is known nor acknowledged, no maintenance is prescribed.
4
Why evidence alone is insufficient
Evidence (bayyinah) and oath cannot be properly established without a contesting party (khasm). Therefore, the judge cannot rely on the claimant's evidence alone.
5
Practical consequence
If the custodian denies either the property or the relationship, the judge cannot order maintenance from the absent's property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب النفقة

أو مديون، أو مضارب إن أقر به، وبالنكاح، أو علم القاضي ذلك وجحد هؤلاء.
ويُكفلها

[1] قوله : إن أقر به ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه إنما تفرض نفقة المذكورين في مال الغائب إن أقر من ماله عنده كالمودع والمضارب والمديون بكون المال عنده، وبأنها زوجته أو أنهما أبواه، أو أنه طفله، فإن أنكر أحدهما فلا فرض وكذا إذا عَلِمَ القاضي بذلك أي بمال وزوجية ونسب.
—
ولو علم بأحدهما احتيج إلى إقرار المودع وغيره بالآخر، فإن لم يعلم القاضي أحدهما ولم يقرّ من ماله عنده بهما أو بأحدهما فلا فرض ؛ لعدم إمكان إثبات ماله أو قرابته بإقامته البينة واليمين ؛ لعدم الخصم. كذا في «البحر» وغيره.

[2] قوله : ويكفلها من التكفيل، بمعنى أخذ الكفيل، كالتحليف بمعنى أخذ الحلف، والتحليف مقدّم على التكفيل، وإن ذكره المصنف له مؤخراً. والحاصل إذا كان للغائب مال عند مودَع وغيره وأقر هو بالنكاح، وبكون ماله عنده ، أو علم القاضي بذلك، وطلبت المرأة أن يفرض القاضي نفقتها من ذلك المال ، حلفها القاضي على أنّ زوجها الغائب لم يعطها النفقة ، فإنّه يحتمل أن يكون زوجها قد أعطاها عند سفره نفقة أشهر عديدة معجّلة فتحلفُ هي ذلك ؛ ليظهر استحقاقها. فإذا حلفـت قـدر لها القاضي النفقة من ذلك المال، وأمر المودع وغيره بدفع ذلك القدر إليها في كل شهر أو كل يوم أو كلّ سنة على حسب ما يرى القاضي، ويأخذ منها الكفيل بالمال، نظراً للغائب ؛ لأنها ربما استوفت نفقتها أو طلقها الزوج وانقضت العدّة.

ويحلفها على أنه لم يعطها النفقة لا بإقامة بينة على النكاح
أي يأخذ منهما كفيلاً) ، ( ويحلّفُها على أنه لم يعطها النفقة)، الضميرُ في أَنه ضمير الغائب، (لا بإقامة" بينة على النكاح (1) (1) : أي لا يفرض القاضي النفقة بإقامة البينة على النكاح ، (ولا) إن لم يُخَلّف مالاً فأقامَتْ بيِّنةً عليه) : أي على النكاح فإذا حضر الزوج فإن صدق المرأةَ وسَلّمَ استحقاقها فذلك، وإن كذَّبَ رجع بماله على الكفيل، ويرجع هو على المرأة، وهكذا الحكم في كل آخذ النفقة من الأولاد والوالدين، كما في «البحر » ) ، فلو ذكر المصنف له الله الضمير لكان أولى، بأن يقول : ويكفله ويحلفه : أي يأخذ الكفيل من آخذ النفقة بعد تحليفه.

بالنفس.

[1]قوله : كفيلاً ؛ أي كفيل بالمال ؛ لأنه هو المقيّد في حق الغائب، لا للكفيل
[2] قوله : ضمير الغائب ؛ هذا يحتمل محملين :
الأول أن يكون المراد به أنّ الضمير في «آنه» للغائب، وليس للشأن. والثاني : أن يكون المراد : إنّ الضمير في «أنه» راجع إلى الغائب المضاف إليه للفظ العرس سابقا.
[3] قوله : لا بإقامة...الخ ؛ يعني إذا كان للغائب مال عند مودع أو غيره فأقرّ بالمالِ وأنكر الزوجية، فأقامت الزوجة بينة على نكاحها به لا يفرضُ القاضي نفقتها عليه بهذه البيئة، ولا يقضي بالنكاح أيضاً ؛ لأنّ البيئة لا تسمح إذا لم يكن هناك خصم حاضر، والقضاء على الغائب لا يجوز، ومثله الحكم في كلّ آخذ التركة إذا أثبت بالبينة لنسبه بالغائب.
[4]قوله : على النكاح ؛ وكذا بإقامة البينة على النسب من غير الزوجة ممن تفرضُ نفقته في مال الغائب.
[5] قوله : ولا ... الخ ؛ أي لا يفرض القاضي النفقة إن لم يترك الغائبُ مالاً. أحد، وأقامت الزوجة بينة على كونها منكوحة ؛ ليقدّر القاضي نفقتها ويأمرها بالاستدانة لا يقضى بذلك لأنه قضاء على الغائب . ينظر : رمز الحقائق (١ : ٢٣٣).
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Maintenance. 

Or a debtor, or a mudarib, if he acknowledges it, and [acknowledges] the marriage, or the judge knows that, and these deny. 
And he takes a guarantor from her.

[1] His statement: 'If he acknowledges it...' etc.; its summary: Maintenance for the mentioned [persons] is only prescribed from the property of the absent if the one who has his property (like the depositary, mudarib, and debtor) acknowledges that the property is with him, and that she is his wife, or that they are his parents, or that he is his child. If one of them denies, then no prescription. Similarly if the judge knows that, i.e., the property, marriage, and lineage.
—
If he knows one of them, the acknowledgment of the depositary and others for the other is needed. If the judge does not know either of them and the one who has his property does not acknowledge both or one of them, then no prescription, because it is impossible to prove his property or relationship by establishing evidence and oath due to the absence of an adversary. As in 'al-Bahr' and others.

[2] His statement: 'And he takes a guarantor from her' from takfil, meaning taking a guarantor, like tahfif meaning taking an oath. Tahfif is given precedence over takfil, even though the author mentioned it later. The summary: If the absent has property with a depositary or other, and he acknowledges the marriage and that his property is with him, or the judge knows that, and the woman asks the judge to prescribe her maintenance from that property, the judge makes her swear that her absent husband did not give her maintenance, because it is possible that her husband gave her an advance maintenance for several months when he traveled, so she swears to that to show her entitlement. When she swears, the judge assesses maintenance for her from that property and orders the depositary and others to pay that amount to her every month or every day or every year according to what the judge sees, and he takes a guarantor from her for the property, out of consideration for the absent, because she might have received her maintenance or the husband might have divorced her and the waiting period ended.

'And he makes her swear that he did not give her maintenance'—not by presenting evidence of marriage—meaning he takes a guarantor from her, and he makes her swear that he (the absent) did not give her maintenance. The pronoun in 'annahu' is a pronoun of the absent. 'Not by presenting evidence of marriage': meaning the judge does not prescribe maintenance by presenting evidence of marriage. 'And not' if he did not leave property and she presented evidence of marriage: i.e., evidence of marriage. If the husband then appears and confirms the woman and acknowledges her entitlement, then that is fine. If he denies, he recovers his property from the guarantor, and the guarantor recovers from the woman. The same ruling applies to every recipient of maintenance from children and parents, as in 'al-Bahr'. If the author had mentioned the pronoun for him, it would have been better, such that he says: 'and he takes a guarantor from him and makes him swear'—i.e., he takes a guarantor from the recipient of maintenance after making him swear.

By himself.

[1] His statement: 'a guarantor': i.e., a guarantor for the property, because it is the thing that is restricted for the absent, not for the guarantor.
[2] His statement: 'pronoun of the absent': this can bear two interpretations: The first: that what is meant is that the pronoun in 'annahu' is for the absent, not for the matter. The second: that what is meant is that the pronoun in 'annahu' refers back to the absent, to which the word 'marriage' was previously added.
[3] His statement: 'Not by presenting...' etc.: meaning if the absent has property with a depositary or other, and he acknowledges the property but denies the marriage, and the wife presents evidence of her marriage to him, the judge does not prescribe maintenance for her from that property through this evidence, and he also does not rule on the marriage, because evidence is not heard when there is no adversary present, and judgment against the absent is not permissible. The same applies to every recipient of the estate who proves his lineage to the absent by evidence.
[4] His statement: 'of marriage': similarly for presenting evidence of lineage for someone other than the wife among those whose maintenance is prescribed from the absent's property.
[5] His statement: 'And not...' etc.: meaning the judge does not prescribe maintenance if the absent did not leave any property, and the wife presented evidence of being married, so that the judge would assess her maintenance and order her to borrow—such a judgment is not given because it is a judgment against the absent. See: Ramz al-Haqa'iq (1:233).
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, clarifies the Hanafi ruling on how maintenance (nafaqah) is granted from the property of an absent husband (gha'ib) when there is a custodian (like a depositary, mudarib, or debtor) who holds the absent's property. The passage type is a detailed ruling with commentary expansion.

The first part states the condition: maintenance is prescribed only if the custodian acknowledges both that the property is with him and the relationship (marriage, parenthood, or childhood) that entitles maintenance. Alternatively, if the judge knows both facts (through his own knowledge, which is authoritative in matters within his jurisdiction), it suffices. If only one is known, the custodian must acknowledge the other. If neither is known nor acknowledged, no maintenance is prescribed because proving property or relationship without a contesting party (khasm) is impossible—evidence (bayyinah) and oath (yamin) cannot be established properly.

The second part explains the procedure: when the conditions are met, the judge first makes the woman swear that her absent husband did not give her maintenance for the period in question. This is to prevent fraud, as the husband might have prepaid maintenance. After the oath, the judge assesses maintenance and orders payment. The judge also takes a guarantor (kafil) from the woman for the property received, to protect the absent's rights. If the husband later appears and confirms her entitlement, it is fine; if he denies, he recovers from the guarantor, who then recovers from the woman.

Important clarifications follow: (a) The judge does not accept evidence (bayyinah) of marriage alone to prescribe maintenance because there is no adversary present to contest the evidence; judgment against the absent is not allowed. (b) If the absent left no property, the judge cannot order the woman to borrow based on her evidence, as this would be a judgment against the absent.

Thus, the Hanafi position prioritizes the absence of a contesting party (khasm) and the need for acknowledgment or judicial knowledge over mere evidence, ensuring the absent's rights are protected. The term 'takfil' (taking a guarantor) and 'tahlif' (administering an oath) are key procedural tools.

Key Terms
غائب (gha'ib): An absent person whose whereabouts are unknown or who is far away, such that direct legal proceedings cannot be conducted with him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid condition for prescribing maintenance from an absent's property?
A) The custodian acknowledges the property and the relationship.
B) The judge knows both the property and the relationship.
C) The woman presents two reliable witnesses to the marriage and the property.
D) The judge knows one fact and the custodian acknowledges the other.

Key Takeaway
Maintenance from an absent's property requires either the custodian's acknowledgment or the judge's knowledge of both property and relationship, not just evidence.

Procedure for Granting Maintenance: Oath and Guarantor
Procedure for oath and guarantor in maintenance from absent's property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the procedural steps a Hanafi judge takes after conditions are met to grant maintenance from an absent's property, including the oath and guarantor, and state their rationale.

Core Idea
The judge must swear the woman and take a guarantor to protect the absent's rights before granting maintenance.

Once the conditions for granting maintenance from an absent's property are satisfied, the judge implements two procedural safeguards. First, the woman must take an oath that her husband has not already paid her maintenance. Second, she must provide a guarantor to ensure repayment if the husband later appears and disputes the debt. This lesson details these steps and their wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Recognize when the procedure applies
The procedure is activated only after the conditions from the previous lesson are met: acknowledgment or judicial knowledge of property and relationship.
2
Administer the oath (tahlif) to the woman
The judge makes the woman swear that her absent husband did not give her maintenance for the period claimed. This prevents fraud if the husband prepaid.
The oath is given precedence over the guarantor in practice.
3
Prescribe maintenance and order payment
After the oath, the judge assesses the maintenance amount and orders the custodian (depositary, etc.) to pay it to the woman periodically (daily, monthly, yearly as per judge's discretion).
4
Take a guarantor (takfil) from the woman
The judge requires the woman to provide a guarantor (kafil) for the property received. This protects the absent's rights in case the husband later appears and denies the debt, or if the marriage ended and iddah expired.
The guarantor is for the property, not for the woman herself.
5
Understand the post-procedure scenario
If the absent husband later appears and confirms the maintenance, no issue. If he denies, he recovers the amount from the guarantor, who then recovers from the woman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب النفقة

أو مديون، أو مضارب إن أقر به، وبالنكاح، أو علم القاضي ذلك وجحد هؤلاء.
ويُكفلها

[1] قوله : إن أقر به ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه إنما تفرض نفقة المذكورين في مال الغائب إن أقر من ماله عنده كالمودع والمضارب والمديون بكون المال عنده، وبأنها زوجته أو أنهما أبواه، أو أنه طفله، فإن أنكر أحدهما فلا فرض وكذا إذا عَلِمَ القاضي بذلك أي بمال وزوجية ونسب.
—
ولو علم بأحدهما احتيج إلى إقرار المودع وغيره بالآخر، فإن لم يعلم القاضي أحدهما ولم يقرّ من ماله عنده بهما أو بأحدهما فلا فرض ؛ لعدم إمكان إثبات ماله أو قرابته بإقامته البينة واليمين ؛ لعدم الخصم. كذا في «البحر» وغيره.

[2] قوله : ويكفلها من التكفيل، بمعنى أخذ الكفيل، كالتحليف بمعنى أخذ الحلف، والتحليف مقدّم على التكفيل، وإن ذكره المصنف له مؤخراً. والحاصل إذا كان للغائب مال عند مودَع وغيره وأقر هو بالنكاح، وبكون ماله عنده ، أو علم القاضي بذلك، وطلبت المرأة أن يفرض القاضي نفقتها من ذلك المال ، حلفها القاضي على أنّ زوجها الغائب لم يعطها النفقة ، فإنّه يحتمل أن يكون زوجها قد أعطاها عند سفره نفقة أشهر عديدة معجّلة فتحلفُ هي ذلك ؛ ليظهر استحقاقها. فإذا حلفـت قـدر لها القاضي النفقة من ذلك المال، وأمر المودع وغيره بدفع ذلك القدر إليها في كل شهر أو كل يوم أو كلّ سنة على حسب ما يرى القاضي، ويأخذ منها الكفيل بالمال، نظراً للغائب ؛ لأنها ربما استوفت نفقتها أو طلقها الزوج وانقضت العدّة.

ويحلفها على أنه لم يعطها النفقة لا بإقامة بينة على النكاح
أي يأخذ منهما كفيلاً) ، ( ويحلّفُها على أنه لم يعطها النفقة)، الضميرُ في أَنه ضمير الغائب، (لا بإقامة" بينة على النكاح (1) (1) : أي لا يفرض القاضي النفقة بإقامة البينة على النكاح ، (ولا) إن لم يُخَلّف مالاً فأقامَتْ بيِّنةً عليه) : أي على النكاح فإذا حضر الزوج فإن صدق المرأةَ وسَلّمَ استحقاقها فذلك، وإن كذَّبَ رجع بماله على الكفيل، ويرجع هو على المرأة، وهكذا الحكم في كل آخذ النفقة من الأولاد والوالدين، كما في «البحر » ) ، فلو ذكر المصنف له الله الضمير لكان أولى، بأن يقول : ويكفله ويحلفه : أي يأخذ الكفيل من آخذ النفقة بعد تحليفه.

بالنفس.

[1]قوله : كفيلاً ؛ أي كفيل بالمال ؛ لأنه هو المقيّد في حق الغائب، لا للكفيل
[2] قوله : ضمير الغائب ؛ هذا يحتمل محملين :
الأول أن يكون المراد به أنّ الضمير في «آنه» للغائب، وليس للشأن. والثاني : أن يكون المراد : إنّ الضمير في «أنه» راجع إلى الغائب المضاف إليه للفظ العرس سابقا.
[3] قوله : لا بإقامة...الخ ؛ يعني إذا كان للغائب مال عند مودع أو غيره فأقرّ بالمالِ وأنكر الزوجية، فأقامت الزوجة بينة على نكاحها به لا يفرضُ القاضي نفقتها عليه بهذه البيئة، ولا يقضي بالنكاح أيضاً ؛ لأنّ البيئة لا تسمح إذا لم يكن هناك خصم حاضر، والقضاء على الغائب لا يجوز، ومثله الحكم في كلّ آخذ التركة إذا أثبت بالبينة لنسبه بالغائب.
[4]قوله : على النكاح ؛ وكذا بإقامة البينة على النسب من غير الزوجة ممن تفرضُ نفقته في مال الغائب.
[5] قوله : ولا ... الخ ؛ أي لا يفرض القاضي النفقة إن لم يترك الغائبُ مالاً. أحد، وأقامت الزوجة بينة على كونها منكوحة ؛ ليقدّر القاضي نفقتها ويأمرها بالاستدانة لا يقضى بذلك لأنه قضاء على الغائب . ينظر : رمز الحقائق (١ : ٢٣٣).
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Maintenance. 

Or a debtor, or a mudarib, if he acknowledges it, and [acknowledges] the marriage, or the judge knows that, and these deny. 
And he takes a guarantor from her.

[1] His statement: 'If he acknowledges it...' etc.; its summary: Maintenance for the mentioned [persons] is only prescribed from the property of the absent if the one who has his property (like the depositary, mudarib, and debtor) acknowledges that the property is with him, and that she is his wife, or that they are his parents, or that he is his child. If one of them denies, then no prescription. Similarly if the judge knows that, i.e., the property, marriage, and lineage.
—
If he knows one of them, the acknowledgment of the depositary and others for the other is needed. If the judge does not know either of them and the one who has his property does not acknowledge both or one of them, then no prescription, because it is impossible to prove his property or relationship by establishing evidence and oath due to the absence of an adversary. As in 'al-Bahr' and others.

[2] His statement: 'And he takes a guarantor from her' from takfil, meaning taking a guarantor, like tahfif meaning taking an oath. Tahfif is given precedence over takfil, even though the author mentioned it later. The summary: If the absent has property with a depositary or other, and he acknowledges the marriage and that his property is with him, or the judge knows that, and the woman asks the judge to prescribe her maintenance from that property, the judge makes her swear that her absent husband did not give her maintenance, because it is possible that her husband gave her an advance maintenance for several months when he traveled, so she swears to that to show her entitlement. When she swears, the judge assesses maintenance for her from that property and orders the depositary and others to pay that amount to her every month or every day or every year according to what the judge sees, and he takes a guarantor from her for the property, out of consideration for the absent, because she might have received her maintenance or the husband might have divorced her and the waiting period ended.

'And he makes her swear that he did not give her maintenance'—not by presenting evidence of marriage—meaning he takes a guarantor from her, and he makes her swear that he (the absent) did not give her maintenance. The pronoun in 'annahu' is a pronoun of the absent. 'Not by presenting evidence of marriage': meaning the judge does not prescribe maintenance by presenting evidence of marriage. 'And not' if he did not leave property and she presented evidence of marriage: i.e., evidence of marriage. If the husband then appears and confirms the woman and acknowledges her entitlement, then that is fine. If he denies, he recovers his property from the guarantor, and the guarantor recovers from the woman. The same ruling applies to every recipient of maintenance from children and parents, as in 'al-Bahr'. If the author had mentioned the pronoun for him, it would have been better, such that he says: 'and he takes a guarantor from him and makes him swear'—i.e., he takes a guarantor from the recipient of maintenance after making him swear.

By himself.

[1] His statement: 'a guarantor': i.e., a guarantor for the property, because it is the thing that is restricted for the absent, not for the guarantor.
[2] His statement: 'pronoun of the absent': this can bear two interpretations: The first: that what is meant is that the pronoun in 'annahu' is for the absent, not for the matter. The second: that what is meant is that the pronoun in 'annahu' refers back to the absent, to which the word 'marriage' was previously added.
[3] His statement: 'Not by presenting...' etc.: meaning if the absent has property with a depositary or other, and he acknowledges the property but denies the marriage, and the wife presents evidence of her marriage to him, the judge does not prescribe maintenance for her from that property through this evidence, and he also does not rule on the marriage, because evidence is not heard when there is no adversary present, and judgment against the absent is not permissible. The same applies to every recipient of the estate who proves his lineage to the absent by evidence.
[4] His statement: 'of marriage': similarly for presenting evidence of lineage for someone other than the wife among those whose maintenance is prescribed from the absent's property.
[5] His statement: 'And not...' etc.: meaning the judge does not prescribe maintenance if the absent did not leave any property, and the wife presented evidence of being married, so that the judge would assess her maintenance and order her to borrow—such a judgment is not given because it is a judgment against the absent. See: Ramz al-Haqa'iq (1:233).
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, clarifies the Hanafi ruling on how maintenance (nafaqah) is granted from the property of an absent husband (gha'ib) when there is a custodian (like a depositary, mudarib, or debtor) who holds the absent's property. The passage type is a detailed ruling with commentary expansion.

The first part states the condition: maintenance is prescribed only if the custodian acknowledges both that the property is with him and the relationship (marriage, parenthood, or childhood) that entitles maintenance. Alternatively, if the judge knows both facts (through his own knowledge, which is authoritative in matters within his jurisdiction), it suffices. If only one is known, the custodian must acknowledge the other. If neither is known nor acknowledged, no maintenance is prescribed because proving property or relationship without a contesting party (khasm) is impossible—evidence (bayyinah) and oath (yamin) cannot be established properly.

The second part explains the procedure: when the conditions are met, the judge first makes the woman swear that her absent husband did not give her maintenance for the period in question. This is to prevent fraud, as the husband might have prepaid maintenance. After the oath, the judge assesses maintenance and orders payment. The judge also takes a guarantor (kafil) from the woman for the property received, to protect the absent's rights. If the husband later appears and confirms her entitlement, it is fine; if he denies, he recovers from the guarantor, who then recovers from the woman.

Important clarifications follow: (a) The judge does not accept evidence (bayyinah) of marriage alone to prescribe maintenance because there is no adversary present to contest the evidence; judgment against the absent is not allowed. (b) If the absent left no property, the judge cannot order the woman to borrow based on her evidence, as this would be a judgment against the absent.

Thus, the Hanafi position prioritizes the absence of a contesting party (khasm) and the need for acknowledgment or judicial knowledge over mere evidence, ensuring the absent's rights are protected. The term 'takfil' (taking a guarantor) and 'tahlif' (administering an oath) are key procedural tools.

Key Terms
تكفيل (takfil): The act of taking a guarantor (kafil) from the recipient of maintenance to ensure repayment if the absent later denies the debt.
تحليف (tahlif): The act of administering an oath to the woman that she has not received maintenance from her absent husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To ensure the woman does not waste the property.
B) To protect the absent's rights in case the woman already received maintenance or the marriage ended.
C) To have a witness for the payment.
D) To guarantee the absent will return.

Key Takeaway
After conditions are met, the judge must swear the woman and take a guarantor to protect the absent's property rights.

Maintenance for Divorced Women: Revocable and Irrevocable
Ruling that maintenance is due to all divorced women during 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that maintenance during 'iddah is wajib for all divorced women, including the irrevocably divorced, and explain the underlying illah of ihtibas using the Quranic proofs.

Core Idea
All divorced women get maintenance during 'iddah as compensation for confinement.

A common misconception is that only revocably divorced women receive maintenance during the waiting period. The Hanafi school, however, holds that even an irrevocably divorced woman (including one divorced three times or before consummation) is entitled to maintenance as long as she is in 'iddah. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and Quranic evidence behind this inclusive ruling, focusing on the concept of ihtibas (confinement) as the effective cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The base text states that maintenance is due to both the revocably and irrevocably divorced woman. The commentator expands this to include all women in 'iddah, regardless of the type of divorce or pregnancy status.
Note the use of absolute terms (mutlaq) to indicate generality.
2
Locate the underlying rationale (illah)
The text explains that maintenance is compensation for ihtibas (confinement) – the woman being confined to the husband's home during marriage and, by extension, during 'iddah. This confinement exists for all divorced women, not just revocable ones.
This is a key Hanafi principle: the illah of ihtibas generates the obligation.
3
Examine the Quranic dalil
Three verses from Surah At-Talaq are cited: 65:1 (do not expel them), 65:6 (lodge them), and 65:6 (spend on them if pregnant). The commentator notes that these verses, by their absolute wording, encompass every divorced woman.
The imperative 'anfiqū' indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
The Hanafi position is mukhtalaf fih: the Shafi'i school holds that an irrevocably divorced non-pregnant woman does not receive maintenance. The disagreement stems from different interpretations of the Quranic verses and the scope of ihtibas.
Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies the Hanafi emphasis on the illah of confinement.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
Consider a woman who is divorced irrevocably (ba'in) after a short marriage. According to Hanafi fiqh, she is entitled to housing and maintenance during her 'iddah of three menstrual cycles, even if she is not pregnant, because she is confined.
This ruling protects the woman's rights during the transition period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمطلقة الرجعي والبائن [1] قوله : والمطلقة الرجعي والبائن ؛ في إطلاق البائن إشارة إلى عموم الحكم المعتدة البائن أعم من أن تكون مطلقة ثلاثاً أو ما دونه ، وفي إطلاق المطلقة إشارة إلى عموم الحكم للحامل وغير الحامل، ولو بدل المطلقة بالمعتدة لكان أولى ؛ لأنّ النفقة منوطة بالعدّة ، ولا نفقة بعد العدة والوجه في ذلك أن النفقة بأنواعها الثلاثة إنما هي جزاء الاحتباس ، وهو كما يوجد في المنكوحة كذلك يوجد في معتدة الطلاق. ويشهد له قوله : وإن كن - أي المطلقات -- أُوْلَتِ حَمْلٍ فَأَنفِقُوا عَلَيْهِنَّ حَتَّى يضَعْنَ حَمَلَهُنَّ ) ، وقوله : تَأَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ إِذَا طَلَقْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ فَطَلِقُوهُنَّ لِعِدَّتِهِنَّ وَأَحْصُوا الْعِدَّةَ وَاتَّقُوا اللَّهَ رَبَّكُمْ لَا تُخْرِجُوهُنَّ مِنْ بُيُوتِهِنَّ ) ، وقوله : أَسْكُنُوهُنَّ مِنْ حيث سكنتُم ) ، فهذه الآيات بإطلاقها يشتمل على كلّ مطلقة.
And the revocably and irrevocably divorced woman. He said: His statement 'the revocably and irrevocably divorced woman' – in the absolute mention of the irrevocably divorced woman is an indication of the generality of the ruling: the waiting woman who is irrevocably divorced, whether she is divorced three times or less. And in the absolute mention of 'divorced woman' is an indication of the generality of the ruling for the pregnant and non-pregnant. And if he had substituted 'divorced woman' with 'waiting woman' it would have been better, because maintenance is tied to the waiting period, and there is no maintenance after the waiting period. And the reasoning is that maintenance in its three types is indeed compensation for confinement (ihtibas), and just as it exists in the married woman, so too it exists in the woman observing the waiting period of divorce. And this is supported by His statement: 'And if they are pregnant, then spend on them until they give birth' (65:6), and His statement: 'O Prophet, when you divorce women, divorce them for their waiting period' (65:1), and His statement: 'Lodge them where you lodge' (65:6). These verses in their absoluteness encompass every divorced woman.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning maintenance (nafaqah) for divorced women. The base text mentions both the revocable (raj'i) and irrevocable (ba'in) divorcée. The commentator explains that the absolute mention of 'ba'in' indicates that the ruling applies to all irrevocably divorced women, whether divorced three times or fewer. Similarly, the term 'divorced woman' includes both pregnant and non-pregnant. The commentator suggests it would be more precise to say 'waiting woman' because maintenance is contingent upon the waiting period ('iddah); after the 'iddah, there is no maintenance. The underlying reason (sabab) is that maintenance is compensation for confinement (ihtibas), i.e., the woman being confined to the husband's home for the 'iddah. This confinement exists in both marriage and the waiting period. The proof is from three Quranic verses: Surah At-Talaq 65:6 (spend on pregnant divorcées until birth), 65:1 (divorce for the waiting period, do not expel them), and 65:6 (lodge them). These verses, by their generality, include all divorced women during 'iddah.

Key Terms
مطلقة رجعي (mutallaqah raj'iyyah): A woman divorced revocably, meaning the husband can take her back during the waiting period without a new marriage contract.
مطلقة بائن (mutallaqah ba'in): A woman divorced irrevocably, where the husband cannot take her back except through a new marriage and the consent of the wife.
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death during which a woman cannot remarry.
احتباس (ihtibas): Confinement or detention; the woman being confined to the husband's home during marriage or 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Hanafi school, why is maintenance due to an irrevocably divorced woman during her waiting period?
A) Because she is still considered a wife until the 'iddah ends.
B) Because maintenance is compensation for confinement (ihtibas), which exists during 'iddah.
C) Because the Quran specifically orders maintenance for all divorced women.
D) Because the husband has the option to take her back.

Key Takeaway
Maintenance during 'iddah is wajib for every divorced woman, revocable or irrevocable, because she is legally confined.

Maintenance for Divorcees and Judicial Practice (Zufar's Opinion)
Explains general ruling on maintenance for divorcees and current judicial practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on maintenance for divorcées during 'idda, identify the Quranic proof, and explain why judges adopt Zufar's opinion for necessity.

Core Idea
Maintenance for all divorcées during 'idda is wajib as compensation for confinement; but judges now follow Zufar's opinion for necessity.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the matn ruling that maintenance is due to both revocable and irrevocably divorced women during their 'idda. He explains that although the standard Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds maintenance wajib for all divorcées, current judicial practice follows the view of Zufar due to practical necessity. This introduces the concept of fatwa based on necessity (darurah) within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is providing maintenance (nafaqah) to a divorced woman during her 'idda. According to the standard Hanafi position, this is wajib.
The ruling applies to both revocable and irrevocable divorcées.
2
Locate the dalil
The Quranic verses (Surah al-Talaq 65:1, 65:6) command lodging and spending on divorcées, which serve as primary proof.
The imperative form indicates obligation per Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'askinuhunna' (lodge them) and the explicit command to spend indicate wujub. The absolute wording covers all divorcées.
Hanafi principle: al-amr yufidu al-wujub.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Zufar holds that non-pregnant irrevocably divorced women do not receive maintenance. However, judges today follow Zufar's view for necessity.
This is one of the twenty issues where fatwa is given on Zufar's opinion.
