The Two Narrations on Hiring a Divorced Mother as a Wet Nurse
Intra-madhhab dispute on hiring a divorced mother for breastfeeding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two narrations within the Hanafi school regarding hiring a divorced mother to breastfeed, identify the relied-upon opinion, and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Two conflicting narrations on hiring a divorced mother: one permits, the other prohibits; the latter is the muftā bihī.

This passage highlights an intra-madhhab ikhtilāf within the Hanafi school. The issue is whether a divorced mother can be hired to breastfeed her own child after the marriage ends. The two narrations reflect different views on whether the marriage bond still affects the ruling. Understanding how the school resolves such disputes is crucial for fatwā practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrations
Note that there are two reports (riwāyatān) from the founding imams: one permits hiring the divorced mother, the other prohibits it.
2
Understand the rationale for each
The first argues that marriage is fully terminated (zāla al-nikāḥ), so no impediment. The second argues that some effects of marriage remain (al-nikāḥ bāqin fī ḥaqq baʿḍ al-aḥkām).
3
Identify which narration is considered Ẓāhir al-Riwāya
Some sources say the first (permissibility) is Ẓāhir al-Riwāya; the second is attributed to Ḥasan ibn Ziyād (rawāya Ḥasan).
This affects the weight in the school.
4
Determine the muftā bihī (relied-upon opinion)
According to al-Tatarkhāniyya and al-Fatḥ al-Qadīr, the second narration (prohibition) is the fatwā. Also indicated by al-Hidāyah and al-Qudūrī.
5
Apply the ruling in practice
Consider a fatwā scenario: a divorced mother seeks payment for breastfeeding. The muftī should rule that it is not permissible, so the father must hire someone else.

دلّ على جملة من المسائل والفوائد على ما بسطه المفسرون في كتبهم :
١. منها : أن لا تكليف شرعاً بما ليس في الوسع ، يؤخذ ذلك من قوله : ﴿ لَا تُكَلِّفُ نَفْسٌ إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا ﴾ - بضم الواو وسكون السين – بمعنى القدرة والطاقة، وهذا أصل كلي تتفرع منه فروع جزئية كثيرة.

Book of Divorce: Maintenance of Relatives. Two narrations. Know that Allah's saying: 'And the mothers shall breastfeed their children...' made breastfeeding obligatory upon mothers. [1] His statement: 'Two narrations': In one narration: It is permissible to hire her because the marriage has ended, just as it is permissible to hire the divorced mother of the child after the waiting period ends. In another narration: No, because the marriage remains in effect regarding some rulings. And it is mentioned in 'al-Jawhara' that the first opinion is more correct, and it is mentioned in 'al-Tatarkhaniyya' that the fatwa is on the second, and he preferred it in 'al-Fath' as well. (Footnote: They are: first: permissibility, some said it is the apparent narration, and the author of al-Jawhara corrected it (2:89). Second: impermissibility, and by it is the narration of Hasan from the Imam, and the words of al-Hidayah (2:46) indicate it, and the apparent words of al-Quduri (p. 82) support it, and in al-Nahr: it is the first, and in al-Tatarkhaniyya: and upon it is the fatwa.) [2] His statement: 'made obligatory'. Know that His statement in Surah al-Baqarah: 'And the mothers shall breastfeed their children for two complete years for those who wish to complete the breastfeeding, and upon the father is their provision and clothing in the known manner. No soul shall be burdened except with its capacity. A mother shall not be harmed because of her child, nor a father because of his child. And upon the heir is similarly. Then if both desire weaning by mutual consent and consultation, there is no sin upon them. And if you wish to seek wet nurses for your children, there is no sin upon you when you hand over what you agreed in the known manner. And fear Allah and know that Allah sees what you do.' Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya, a supercommentary on Sharḥ al-Wiqāya, deals with the legal rulings (aḥkām) derived from the Quranic verse on breastfeeding (Āyat al-Raḍā', al-Baqarah 2:233) and a related intra-madhhab dispute on hiring a divorced mother as a wet nurse. The commentator notes that al-Jawhara al-Nayyira declared the first narration more correct, but al-Tatarkhāniyya and al-Fatḥ al-Qadīr state that the fatwa is on the second (prohibition). The footnote adds that the first is considered Ẓāhir al-Riwāya by some, while the second is the narration of Ḥasan ibn Ziyād from the Imam, and that the latter is the operative position. The commentator then proceeds to extract seven legal benefits from the verse. Third, that the maximum breastfeeding period is two complete years (ḥawlān kāmilān), and breastfeeding after that is not valid, supported by the verse in Sūrat al-Aḥqāf. Fourth, that weaning before two years is permissible with mutual parental consent and consultation. Fifth, that hiring wet nurses is permissible, derived from 'wa in aradtum an tastarḍiʿū'. Seventh, the prohibition of parents harming each other (ḥurmat muḍārrat al-wālidayn), derived from 'lā tuḍārra'. The passage demonstrates the method of istinbāṭ (derivation) from the Qur'an, using linguistic indications and contextual reasoning.

Key Terms
روایتان (riwāyatān): Two narrations (transmissions) within the Ḥanafī school regarding a particular ruling
ديانة (diyānatan): Religious/moral obligation, as opposed to legal enforceability (qaḍā'an). The mother is sinful if she refuses, but the state does not compel her.
إجارة المراضع (ijārat al-marāḍiʿ): Hiring wet nurses (women to breastfeed children)
ظاهر الرواية (Ẓāhir al-Riwāya): The 'apparent' or well-known transmission from the founding imams, recorded in the six core books.
فتوى (fatwā): The legal opinion given to a question, often the relied-upon position in the school.

Talaq — Divorce

Hiring a Divorced Woman for Breastfeeding After 'Iddah
Ruling on hiring a divorced woman for breastfeeding after 'iddah with evidence and reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hiring a divorced woman for breastfeeding after her 'iddah, identify the Quranic evidence, and explain why it is permissible only after 'iddah.

Core Idea
A divorced woman may be hired for breastfeeding after 'iddah because maintenance ends; wages become obligatory.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a specific fiqh scenario: after a divorce and completion of 'iddah, can a father hire his ex-wife to breastfeed their child? The author confirms it is valid and provides the legal reasoning distinguishing between the 'iddah period and after. We will walk through the ruling, the objection, and the Quranic proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage states it is valid (sahih) to hire a divorced woman for breastfeeding her child after her 'iddah, or for a child from another wife. The ruling is that hiring is permissible and paying wages is obligatory.
This applies only after 'iddah; during 'iddah it is not valid.
2
Understand the potential objection
A reader might object: if breastfeeding is obligatory on mothers by text, why should wages be due? The author anticipates this and rebuts it.
The objection is based on Surah Al-Baqarah 2:233 which implies obligation.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
The rebuttal distinguishes two periods: during marriage or 'iddah, maintenance (nafaqah) is already obligatory on the father, so no extra payment is due. After 'iddah, maintenance ceases, so wages become obligatory as compensation.
This is the key legal reasoning (ta'lil).
4
Locate the Quranic evidence
The author cites Surah At-Talaq 65:6: 'Then if they breastfeed for you, give them their wages.' This verse is explicit about paying wages to divorced mothers who breastfeed after 'iddah.
The imperative 'fa'atūhunna' indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.
5
Note the extension to other children
The ruling also applies if the woman is hired to breastfeed a child from another wife of the father. This is valid even if she is still in 'iddah from her own marriage, as she has no obligation to breastfeed that child.
The passage mentions 'or for his son from another woman' to cover this.

والجواب عنه : إن الزوج وإن أو حشها لكن ما دامت في العدة لا تنقطع عنه بالكلية، ونفقتها واجبة عليه، فلا تجب بالإرضاع شيء آخر إذا أقدمت عليه، بخلاف ما بعد
العدة.
[١] قوله : وإنما تجوز...الخ ؛ دفعُ دخل مقدّر، تقدير الدخل : أنهم قد صرحوا بجواز الإجارة بعد انقضاء العدة، ووجوب إعطاء الإجرة عليه، مع أن الإرضاع واجب على الأمهات بالنص، فينبغي أن لا تجب الأجرة.
وحاصل الدفع : إنّ الشرع كما أوجب الإرضاع على الأمهات، كذلك أوجب على الآباءِ رزقُ المرضعات وكسوتهن بالمعروف ، ولَمَّا كانت المنكوحة والمعتدة تجب نفقتهن وكسوتهن عليه بالعدّة أو النكاح لا يجب عليه شيء آخر للإرضاع، فلا تجوز استئجارهن له، فإذا انقضت العدّة وصارت أجنبية من الأب لا تجب على الأب نفقتها وكسوتها ، فإذا أقدمت على الإرضاع تجب عليه نفقتها بالمعروف، فلذلك جاز
استئجارها له.
[٢]قوله : لقوله ، ولقوله لا في سورة الطلاق : فَإِنْ أَرْضَعْنَ لَكُمْ فَأْتُوهُنَّ (۱)، والاستدلال بهذا أولى من الاستدلال بالآية السابقة، فإن آية سورة الطلاق نص في جواز استئجار الأمهات التي انقضت عدَّتهنَّ، ووجوب إعطاء أجرتهنّ
عليه.
[٣]قوله : أو لابنه ؛ أي لإرضاعِ ابنِهِ المولود من زوجة أخرى غير هذه الزوجة. [٤] قوله : لإرضاع ولده الذي منها ؛ أي الذي ولد من رحمها، ويعلم منه حكمُ استئجار التي انقضت عدتها لإرضاع ابنه الذي من غيرها بالطريق الأولى.
And for breastfeeding him after the 'iddah or for his son from another woman, it is valid. And hiring is only permissible after the 'iddah because maintenance is not obligatory for her, so wages become obligatory; due to His statement: 'And upon the father is their provision and clothing.' [٢][٣] 'And for breastfeeding him after the 'iddah or for his son from another woman, it is valid' means hiring for breastfeeding her child from him after he divorced her and her 'iddah expired. The answer to it: although the husband may have alienated her, as long as she is in 'iddah she is not completely disconnected from him, and her maintenance is obligatory upon him, so nothing additional is due for breastfeeding if she undertakes it, unlike after the 'iddah. [١] His statement: 'And hiring is only permissible...' is a rebuttal of a potential objection: the objection is that they have declared hiring permissible after the 'iddah and the obligation of paying wages, even though breastfeeding is obligatory upon mothers by text, so wages should not be obligatory. The outcome of the rebuttal: just as the Law obligated breastfeeding upon mothers, so too it obligated upon fathers the provision and clothing of wet nurses in a known manner. Since the wife and the woman in 'iddah have their maintenance and clothing obligatory upon him due to marriage or 'iddah, nothing else is due for breastfeeding; so hiring them for it is not permissible. When the 'iddah ends and she becomes a stranger to the father, no maintenance or clothing is obligatory upon the father, so if she undertakes breastfeeding, her maintenance becomes obligatory upon him in a known manner; therefore hiring her for it is valid. [٢] His statement: 'due to His statement' and it is in Surah At-Talaq: 'Then if they breastfeed for you, give them their wages.' (1) And using this as evidence is stronger than using the previous verse, for the verse of Surah At-Talaq is explicit on the permissibility of hiring mothers whose 'iddah has expired and the obligation of giving them their wages. [٣] His statement: 'or for his son' means for breastfeeding his son born from another wife other than this wife. [٤] His statement: 'for breastfeeding her child from him' means the child born from her womb; from this is known the ruling of hiring a woman whose 'iddah has expired for breastfeeding his son from another woman, by primary analogy.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn regarding the permissibility of hiring a divorced woman for breastfeeding after her 'iddah. The author begins by restating the ruling: it is valid (sahih) to hire a woman for breastfeeding her own child from that marriage after the 'iddah, or for a child from another wife. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: if breastfeeding is obligatory on mothers, why should wages be due? The answer is that during the marriage or 'iddah, maintenance is already obligatory, so no additional payment is required. After 'iddah, no maintenance is due, so wages become obligatory. The proof is from the Quran (Surah At-Talaq 65:6) which commands giving wages to wet nurses. The commentary also clarifies that hiring for a child from another wife is valid even if the woman is still married or in 'iddah, because she has no obligation to breastfeed that child. The explanation follows the Hanafi school's reasoning, distinguishing between obligations during marriage/iddah and after.

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ijārah): Contract of hiring services for a wage.
العدة (al-ʿiddah): Waiting period after divorce during which marriage is not permitted.
الإرضاع (al-irdāʿ): Breastfeeding of an infant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because she is not obligated to breastfeed after 'iddah.
B) Because during 'iddah, maintenance is already obligatory, so no extra payment is due.
C) Because the father has no right to hire her during 'iddah.
D) Because the child is not entitled to breast milk after divorce.

Key Takeaway
Hiring a divorced woman for breastfeeding is valid only after 'iddah; wages are obligatory based on Quran 65:6.

Mother's Precedence in Breastfeeding Over a Stranger
Ruling on mother's right to nurse her child before a stranger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mother's right of precedence in breastfeeding and identify the condition under which a stranger takes precedence.

Core Idea
The mother's right to nurse her child is conditional; she loses precedence if she demands a wage higher than a stranger.

In Hanafi fiqh, the mother has a preferential right to breastfeed her child over any hired wet nurse. However, this right is conditional. This lesson examines the precise condition that can shift precedence to a stranger, and the Quranic basis for this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states: 'She (the mother) has more right than a stranger.' This establishes the default precedence of the mother over an alien wet nurse.
The ruling is about the right to nurse, not an obligation.
2
Locate the condition
The exception: 'unless she demands an increased wage.' The mother loses her precedence if she asks for a wage higher than what the stranger would accept.
If the mother demands the same or less, she retains precedence.
3
Trace the dalil
The Quranic verse (Al-Baqarah 2:233) prohibits harming the mother. Demanding an excessively high wage is considered harming the father, so the stranger is preferred.
The dalil is referenced but not quoted fully in the text.
4
Apply to different wage scenarios
Compare the mother's demanded wage to the stranger's: if equal or less → mother has precedence; if more → stranger has precedence.
Also, if the mother demands a wage and the stranger volunteers for free, the stranger is preferred.
5
Note the scope
The ruling applies specifically to a divorced mother after her 'iddah has expired. A wife or a woman in 'iddah cannot be hired, so the precedence rule does not apply.
During 'iddah, the mother is ineligible to be a wet nurse for hire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي أحق من الأجنبية إلا إذا طلبت زيادة أجرة
(وهي) : أي الأم أحق من الأجنبية إلا إذا طلبت" زيادة أجرة. ونفقة البنت بالغة والابن زَمِناً ) على الأب خاصة ، به يُفتى)
[٢]قوله : أحق ؛ أي أم الولد التي تقدم على إرضاع ولده منها أحق من الأجنبية بإرضاع ولدها، فتقدم عليها إذا لم تطلب أجراً ، أو طلبت مثل ما تطلب الأجنبية أو أدون منه ، وأما إذا طلبت أجرة أزيد مما تطلبه الظئر الأجنبية فالأجنبية أولى، وكذا إذا طلبت الأم الأجر وتبرعت الأجنبية به، وهذا كله مستنبط من قوله : لَا تُضَار وَالِدَة بولدها وَلَا مَوْلُودٌ لَهُ يوليو (٢)
[٣]قوله : إلا إذا طلبت ؛ أي الأم، وهذا الاستثناء يرشدك إلى أن حكم الأحقية المذكور سابقاً في الأم المطلقة التي انقضت عدتها ، فإنّ المنكوحة والمعتدة لا يجوز استئجارها ، فلا معنى بطلب أجرتها زيادة على ما تطلبه الأجنبية.
She (the mother) has more right than a stranger unless she demands an increased wage. (She says: That is, the mother has more right than a stranger unless she demands an increased wage. And the maintenance of an adult daughter and a disabled son is especially upon the father; this is the fatwa.) [٢] His statement: 'more right' means the mother of the child who comes forward to breastfeed her child from him has more right than a stranger to breastfeed her child, so she is preferred if she does not ask for a wage, or asks for the same as the stranger or less. But if she asks for a wage greater than what the foreign wet nurse asks, then the stranger is more entitled. Likewise if the mother asks for a wage and the stranger volunteers to do it for free, all this is derived from His statement: 'A mother shall not be harmed on account of her child, nor a father on account of his child.' (2) [٣] His statement: 'unless she demands' means the mother, and this exception indicates that the ruling of precedence mentioned earlier applies to the divorced mother whose 'iddah has expired, for the wife and woman in 'iddah cannot be hired; so there is no meaning to asking for her wage increased over what the stranger asks.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on the mother's priority to nurse her child over a hired wet nurse. The matn states she is more entitled (ahaqq) unless she demands a higher wage. The commentary explains that the mother is preferred if she asks for no wage, or less than or equal to the stranger's wage. If she asks for more, the stranger takes precedence. This is derived from the Quranic principle of not harming the mother or father. The exception clarifies that this ruling applies specifically to the divorced mother after 'iddah; a wife or woman in 'iddah cannot be hired at all, so the issue of demanding higher wages does not arise.

Key Terms
أحقية (ahaqqiyyah): Precedence or stronger right to perform an act.
الظئر (al-zi'r): A wet nurse (stranger hired for breastfeeding).

Divorce by Word of Manumission: The Majaz Argument
Defends Hanafi ruling that 'a'taqtuki' effects talaq via majaz mursal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that saying 'a'taqtuki' to a free wife with the intention of divorce effects a valid talaq, based on the principle of majaz mursal.

Core Idea
The word for manumission can effect divorce because it is the cause of the removal of marital ownership — a relationship justifying metaphorical usage.

Can a husband divorce his free wife by simply saying 'I have freed you'? According to the Hanafi school, yes — but only if he intends divorce. This ruling may seem puzzling at first, but it is grounded in a sophisticated linguistic principle: majaz mursal, where a word is used metaphorically due to a causal relationship between its original and intended meanings. In this lesson, we unpack the three preliminary principles that justify this metaphorical transfer and address an intra-Hanafi objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the dispute
The Hanafi school rules that saying 'a'taqtuki' with the intention of divorce effects a valid talaq. Some later Hanafi scholars, like al-Talwih, considered this view weak. The passage defends the majority position.
The ruling applies to free wives; the metaphorical extension is from the case of slave wives where manumission automatically ends the marriage.
2
Understand the three preliminaries of majaz
The author explains (1) the definition of metaphor (majaz) and the necessity of a relationship (alaqa) between original and metaphorical meanings; (2) the specific nature of majaz mursal, which uses the name of the necessary condition (malzum) for the necessary consequence (lazim); (3) when originality is mutual or one-sided, metaphor can be bidirectional or unilateral.
Majaz mursal is distinguished from isti'ara (simile-based metaphor). The relationship here is causality (sababiyya).
3
Apply the preliminaries to the case
Manumission (removal of ownership of the person) is the cause of divorce (removal of marital ownership) in the case of a slave wife. This causal relationship is generalizable: removing ownership of the person is a cause for removing marital ownership in general. Therefore, the word for the cause can be used metaphorically for the effect, even for a free wife.
The relationship is one-sided (manumission causes divorce, not vice versa), so metaphor is only from cause to effect, not the reverse.
4
Evaluate the objection and the Hanafi response
Al-Talwih objected that the metaphorical transfer is not proven by actual linguistic transmission (naql) and is therefore weak. The commentator in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya dismisses this, asserting that the causal relationship is sufficient for majaz mursal, and notes that the majority of Hanafi scholars ('they agreed') uphold the ruling.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is whether a divorce occurs; the mufta bihi is that it does.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view in the Hanafi school is that saying 'a'taqtuki' with the intention of divorce effects a valid talaq. You should be able to articulate the linguistic reasoning behind this ruling.
This ruling is an example of how Hanafi usul al-fiqh uses majaz mursal to extend rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فيجري المجاز...الخ ؛ توضيحه : يقتضي تمهيد مقدمات : الأولى : إن المجاز عبارة عن لفظ يستعمل في غير الموضوع له، ولا بد فيه من العلاقة بين المعنى الأصلي وبين المعنى المجازي ؛ ليصح استعمال اللفظ الموضوع للأوّل في الثاني، فإنّه لو لم تعتبر تلك العلاقة يكون ذلك الاستعمال غلطاً، كاستعمال الأرض في السماء. وتلك العلاقة إن كانت علاقة التشبيه فيسمى حينئذ استعارة، كإطلاق الأسد على الإنسان الشجاع ؛ لعلاقة اشتراكهما في وصف الشجاعة، وقصد تشبيه المستعار له أي الإنسان الشجاع بالمستعار منه ؛ أي الأسد. وإن كانت غير ذلك يسمّى مجازاً مرسلاً، وينقسم المجاز المرسل بحسب تنوّع العلاقات إلى أقسام كثيرة، كإطلاق الجزء على الكلّ، وإطلاق السبب على المسبب، والحال على المحل، وبالعكس إلى غير ذلك مما هو مبسوط في كتب أصول الفقه، وكتب علم المعاني والبيان. (1) من «التلويح»(١ : ١٥٠ – ١٥١). الثانية : إن مبنى المجاز المرسل على إطلاق اسم الملزوم على اللازم، فالملزوم أصل، واللازم فرع ، وليس المراد باللزوم هاهنا ما يذكره المنطقيون من امتناع الانفكاك، بل مجرد انتقال الذهن من شيء إلى شيء ؛ لمناسبة باعثة عليه، كعلاقة الحلول والعروض السببية وغيرهما. من هاهنا وضح عندك أنّ التعريف الذي ذكره الشارح له سابقا بقوله : المجاز ؛ لفظ يذكر ويراد به لازمه، ليس تعريفاً لمطلق المجاز، بحيث يصدق على نوعيه، بل لأحد نوعيه، وهو المجاز المرسل الذي يتعلّق البحث به، فإنّ استعمال الطلاق في العتاق وبالعكس إنما هو من قبيل المجازات المرسلة. الثالثة : إنهم صرحوا بأنّ الأصلية والفرعيّة إن كانت من الجانبين بأن يكون بين الشيئين، بحيث يكون كلّ منهما أصلاً من وجه، وفرعاً من وجه، ففي هذه الصورة يجري المجاز من الطرفين ؛ إذ المجاز استعمال لفظ الأصل في الفرع ، فلما كان كل منهما أصلاً وفرعاً صح استعمال لفظ كل منهما في الآخر. ونظيره اتصال العلة مع المعلول الذي هو علّة غائيّة لها ، فالعلة أصل من جهة احتياج المعلول إليه، والمعلولُ أصل من جهة كونه بمنزلة العلة الغائية للعلة، ومقصوداً منها، فيصح إطلاق اسم العلة على المعلول وبالعكس، وكذا اتصال الكل والجزء، فإنّ الجزء أصل من حيث توقف الكلّ عليه، والكلّ أصل من حيث كونه مقصوداً منه ، فيصح إطلاق اسم الكلّ على الجزء، وبالعكس. وإن كانت الأصلية والفرعية من جانب واحد جاز المجاز من جانب واحد بإطلاق اسم الأصل على الفرع دون العكس، كما في علاقة السببية المحضة التي ليست في معنى العلية. إذا انتفشت على صفحة خاطرك هذه المقدّمات فاعرف : إنهم اتفقوا على وقوع الطلاق بلفظ العتق ، بأن يقول لزوجته : أعتقتك مريداً طلاقها، بناء على أنّ الإعتاق موضوع لإزالة ملك الرقبة، والطلاق لإزالة ملك المتعة والإزالة الثانية لازمة للإزالة الأولى، فإنّه عند زوال ملك الرقبة عن الأمة يزولُ ملك المتعة، فتكون إزالة ملك الرقبة سبباً لإزالة ملك المتعة في الجملة، فيصح أن يطلق لفظ السبب وهو الإعتاق، ويراد به لازمه ومسبه وهو مفاد الطلاق.
His statement: "So the metaphor occurs..." etc. Explanation: It requires preparation of preliminaries: First: Metaphor (majaz) is a word used in a meaning other than its original, and there must be a relationship between the original meaning and the metaphorical meaning for the usage to be valid; otherwise, it would be erroneous, like using 'earth' for 'sky'. If the relationship is that of similarity, it is called isti'ara, like applying 'lion' to a brave man due to shared bravery, intending to compare the brave man to the lion. If not, it is called majaz mursal, divided into many types based on relationships, like part for whole, cause for effect, state for place, and vice versa, as detailed in books of usul al-fiqh and balaghah. (From al-Talwih 1:150-151). Second: Majaz mursal is based on using the name of the necessary condition (malzum) for the necessary consequence (lazim). Malzum is original, lazim is derivative. They do not mean logical inseparability here, but merely mental transition due to a motivating suitability, like the relationship of inherence, causation, etc. From this it becomes clear that the definition given by the commentator earlier: 'Metaphor: a word mentioned meaning its necessary consequence' is not a definition of absolute metaphor encompassing both types, but only one type, namely majaz mursal which is the subject of discussion, because using divorce for manumission and vice versa is of this type. Third: They explicitly stated: If originality and derivativeness are mutual, i.e., each of two things is original in one respect and derivative in another, then metaphor occurs from both sides, because metaphor is using the word of the original for the derivative, and since each is original and derivative, it is valid to use the word of each for the other. An analogy is the connection of cause with effect, where the effect is the final cause; the cause is original in that the effect depends on it, and the effect is original in that it is like the final cause of the cause and intended by it; so it is valid to use the name of the cause for the effect and vice versa. Similarly, the connection of whole and part: the part is original in that the whole depends on it, and the whole is original in that it is intended by the part; so it is valid to use the name of the whole for the part and vice versa. If originality and derivativeness are one-sided, metaphor is allowed from one side only, using the name of the original for the derivative but not vice versa, as in pure causation that is not in the sense of final cause. When these preliminaries are settled, know: They agreed on the occurrence of divorce by the word of manumission, i.e., if one says to his wife 'I have freed you' intending divorce, based on the fact that 'i'taq' is originally used for removal of ownership of the slave's person, and divorce is for removal of marital ownership; the second removal is a necessary consequence of the first, because when ownership of the slave's person is removed from a slave woman, marital ownership is removed. So removal of ownership of the person is a cause for removal of marital ownership in general. Thus it is valid to use the word for the cause (manumission) and intend its necessary consequence and effect, i.e., the meaning of divorce.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending the Hanafi ruling that if a husband says to his wife 'a'taqtuki' (I have freed you) with the intention of divorcing her, a divorce occurs. The author explains the linguistic foundation of this ruling through the theory of metaphorical usage (majaz). He begins with three preliminaries: (1) the definition of metaphor and the necessity of a relationship (علاقة) between original and figurative meanings; (2) the specific nature of majaz mursal, which uses the term for the necessary condition to mean the necessary consequence, not logical inseparability but mere mental association; (3) when both terms have mutual original and derivative aspects, metaphor can occur in both directions, like cause and effect or whole and part. Applying this to manumission and divorce: manumission removes ownership of the slave's person, and divorce removes marital ownership. In the case of a slave wife, the two removals are linked—freeing her automatically ends the marriage. For a free wife, though not directly, the relationship is one of cause and effect: manumission is the cause of removal of marital ownership in some cases, so by extension the word can be used metaphorically for divorce. The Hanafi school holds that this metaphorical usage is valid because there is a clear relationship (علاقة السببية) between the two meanings. The author cites the objection from al-Talwih that this metaphorical transfer is not proven by actual usage (naql) and is weak, but he dismisses this and reaffirms the accepted position that divorce does occur.

Key Terms
مجاز مرسل (majaz mursal): A type of metaphor where the relationship between original and figurative meaning is not similarity but another connection like causality, part-whole, etc.
علاقة (alaqa): The relationship or connection between the original meaning and the metaphorical meaning that justifies the figurative usage.
سبب (sabab): Cause; in this context, manumission is described as the cause (sabab) that leads to the effect (musabbab) of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says to his free wife 'a'taqtuki' intending divorce, what is the ruling?
A) No divorce occurs because the word is only for manumission.
B) A divorce occurs because of the metaphorical relationship between manumission and divorce.
C) It is considered a valid divorce only if the wife was a slave.
D) The husband must repeat the word with the explicit intention of divorce for it to take effect.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'a'taqtuki' to a free wife with the intention of divorce effects a valid talaq according to the Hanafi school, based on the principle of majaz mursal where the cause (manumission) stands for the effect (divorce).

The One-Way Metaphor: Manumission and Divorce
Analyzing the validity of using manumission terms for divorce and vice versa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi theory of majāz mursal to determine whether a word used for divorce can effect manumission, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter‑argument using isti āra.

Core Idea
Manumission implies divorce, but divorce does not imply manumission — so metaphor works one way only.

This lesson explores a subtle but critical rule in Hanafi fiqh: the direction of legal metaphor. Using the example of a husband who says 'I divorce you' to his slave‑wife intending to free her, we will see why the Hanafi school holds that manumission does not occur, while the Shafi i school disagrees. The key lies in understanding causality and the nature of majāz mursal.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly what the Hanafi school rules about using divorce words for manumission and vice versa.
The ruling is asymmetric: divorce occurs by the word 'itq' (with intent), but manumission does NOT occur by the word 'talāq'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The opposing school is al‑Shafi i. Their view is that manumission can be effected by the word 'talāq' because of a metaphorical link.
Their reasoning may rely on isti āra (simile‑based metaphor) rather than causality.
3
Follow the opponent’s proof
Understand how the Shafi i school justifies using 'talāq' for 'itq': they argue that both involve ending a relationship, so there is a basis for metaphor.
They attempt to avoid the one‑way causality limitation by invoking similarity.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal shows that the required well‑known attribute (wasf mashhūr) for isti āra is missing, and the causality‑based majāz is one‑directional.
Intention (niyyah) cannot repair an invalid linguistic use.
5
Determine the thamarah al‑khilāf
The practical outcome: under Hanafi law, a man who says 'I divorce you' to his slave‑wife with intent to free her does not free her; she remains a slave. Under Shafi i law, she would be freed.
This difference has real consequences in family and slave law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : إن مبنى المجاز المرسل على إطلاق اسم الملزوم على اللازم، فالملزوم أصل، واللازم فرع ، وليس المراد باللزوم هاهنا ما يذكره المنطقيون من امتناع الانفكاك، بل مجرد انتقال الذهن من شيء إلى شيء ؛ لمناسبة باعثة عليه، كعلاقة الحلول والعروض السببية وغيرهما.
من هاهنا وضح عندك أنّ التعريف الذي ذكره الشارح له سابقا بقوله : المجاز ؛ لفظ يذكر ويراد به لازمه، ليس تعريفاً لمطلق المجاز، بحيث يصدق على نوعيه، بل لأحد نوعيه، وهو المجاز المرسل الذي يتعلّق البحث به، فإنّ استعمال الطلاق في العتاق وبالعكس إنما هو من قبيل المجازات المرسلة.
الاتصال
الثالثة : إنهم صرحوا بأنّ الأصلية والفرعيّة إن كانت من الجانبين بأن يكون بين الشيئين، بحيث يكون كلّ منهما أصلاً من وجه، وفرعاً من وجه، ففي هذه الصورة يجري المجاز من الطرفين ؛ إذ المجاز استعمال لفظ الأصل في الفرع ، فلما كان كل منهما أصلاً وفرعاً صح استعمال لفظ كل منهما في الآخر.
ونظيره اتصال العلة مع المعلول الذي هو علّة غائيّة لها ، فالعلة أصل من جهة احتياج المعلول إليه، والمعلولُ أصل من جهة كونه بمنزلة العلة الغائية للعلة، ومقصوداً منها، فيصح إطلاق اسم العلة على المعلول وبالعكس، وكذا اتصال الكل والجزء، فإنّ الجزء أصل من حيث توقف الكلّ عليه، والكلّ أصل من حيث كونه مقصوداً منه ، فيصح إطلاق اسم الكلّ على الجزء، وبالعكس.
وإن كانت الأصلية والفرعية من جانب واحد جاز المجاز من جانب واحد بإطلاق اسم الأصل على الفرع دون العكس، كما في علاقة السببية المحضة التي ليست في معنى العلية.
إذا انتفشت على صفحة خاطرك هذه المقدّمات فاعرف : إنهم اتفقوا على وقوع الطلاق بلفظ العتق ، بأن يقول لزوجته : أعتقتك مريداً طلاقها، بناء على أنّ الإعتاق موضوع لإزالة ملك الرقبة، والطلاق لإزالة ملك المتعة والإزالة الثانية لازمة للإزالة الأولى، فإنّه عند زوال ملك الرقبة عن الأمة يزولُ ملك المتعة، فتكون إزالة ملك الرقبة سبباً لإزالة ملك المتعة في الجملة، فيصح أن يطلق لفظ السبب وهو الإعتاق، ويراد به لازمه ومسبه وهو مفاد الطلاق.
وأما وقوع العتق بلفظ : الطلاق ؛ فيصح عند الشافعي ، ولا يصح عندنا ، وذلك لما مهدنا سابقاً أن المجاز الذي يكون بسبب علاقة اللزومِ والسببية إنما يصح من الطرفين إذا كانت السببية في معنى العلّة، وتوجد الملازمة، فيكون كل منهما أصلاً من وجه، وفرعاً من وجه، لا في السببية المحضة.
ومن المعلوم أن إزالة ملك الرقبة سبب محض لإزالة ملك المتعة، وليست علّة مؤثرة فيها، فكثيراً ما توجد إزالة ملك الرقبة من دون أن توجد هناك إزالة ملك المتعة، كما في عنق العبيد وعتق الإماء التي هي من محارم المعتق، أو الإماء المزوجة ؛ لعدم وجود ملك المتعة في هذه الصور حتى يزول بزوال ملك الرقبة.
وبالجملة : إزالة ملك الرقبة وإن كان سبباً لإزالة ملك المتعة لكنَّ الإزالة الثانية ليست بمقصودة منها ، وإزالة ملك المتعة وإن كانت لازمة لإزالة ملك الرقبة، لكن إزالة ملك الرقبة ليست لازمة لإزالة ملك المتعة، فلا يصح المجاز هاهنا من الطرف الآخر، وإنما يصح إطلاق اسم السبب وهو العتق على المسبب لا العكس، حتى يقع العتقُ بلفظ الطلاق صريحاً كان أو كناية.
فإن قلت : ينبغي أن يقع العتق بلفظ : الطلاق مع النية.
قلت : النيّة إنّما تعتبرُ بعد صحة استعمال اللفظ في المعنى المجازي، وهاهنا لا صحة لاستعمال لفظ الطلاق في العتاق لا حقيقة ولا مجازاً، فلا عبرة للنية، كما إذا نوى الأرض من لفظ السماء.
ثم اعلم أنه قد يوجه مذهب الشافعي له بأنه يمكن أن يطلق الطلاق ويراد به العتق على طريق الاستعارة المبنية على علاقة التشبيه لا على طريق المجاز المرسل بعلاقة السببية والمسببية، حتى يقال بعدم صحة المجاز من الطرفين.
وأجيب عنه : تارةً بأن الاستعارة لا تصح بكل وصف للقطع بامتناع استعارة السماء للأرض مع اشتراكهما في الوجود والحدوث، بل لا بد من وصف مشهور له زيادة اختصاص بالمستعار منه كشجاعة الأسد، وهذا غير متحقق في الطلاق والعتاق ؛ فإن العتاق عبارة عن إثباتِ القوّة المخصوصة، والطلاق عن إزالة القيد، فلا تشابه بين المعنيين في الوجه الذي شرعا عليه.
Second point: The basis of al-majaz al-mursal is using the name of the necessary thing (al-malzum) for its necessary consequence (al-lazim). Al-malzum is the original, and al-lazim is the derivative. What is meant by necessity here is not what the logicians mention of impossibility of separation, but rather the mere transfer of the mind from one thing to another due to a motivating appropriateness, such as the relation of inherence and occurrence, causality, and others. Hence, it is clear to you that the definition which the commentator (Sharh) gave earlier: 'al-majaz is a word used and its necessary consequence is intended' is not a definition of absolute majaz such that it applies to both types, but rather to one type: al-majaz al-mursal which is the subject of discussion. For using 'talaq' for 'itaq' and vice versa is of the type of al-majaz al-mursal. Connection. Third point: They explicitly stated that if originality and derivative nature are from both sides, such that between two things each is original in one respect and derivative in another, then in this case majaz is permissible from both sides. Since majaz is using the word of the original for the derivative, when each is both original and derivative, it is valid to use the word of each for the other. Its analogy is the connection of the cause with the effect which is its final cause. The cause is original in that the effect depends on it, and the effect is original in that it is like the final cause of the cause and its intended purpose, so it is valid to use the name of the cause for the effect and vice versa. Similarly, the connection of the whole and the part. The part is original in that the whole depends on it, and the whole is original in that it is intended from it, so it is valid to use the name of the whole for the part and vice versa. If originality and derivative nature are from one side only, majaz is permissible from one side only: using the name of the original for the derivative but not vice versa, as in a purely causal relationship that is not in the nature of real causation (illiyyah). Now that these premises have been settled in your mind, you should know: They agreed that divorce occurs by the word of manumission ('itq), when a man says to his wife, 'I have freed you,' intending divorce. This is based on the fact that 'itaq is prescribed for removing ownership of the slave's person (ra'aba), while divorce is for removing ownership of pleasure (mut'a). The second removal is a necessary consequence of the first removal. For when ownership of the slave's person is removed from a female slave, ownership of pleasure also goes away. Thus, removal of ownership of the slave's person becomes a cause for removal of ownership of pleasure in general, so it is valid to utter the word of the cause, which is 'itaq, and intend its necessary consequence and effect, which is the meaning of divorce. As for the occurrence of manumission by the word 'talaq': it is valid according to al-Shafi'i, but not valid according to us (Hanafis). This is because, as we have previously established, the majaz that is due to the relation of necessity and causality is only valid from both sides when the causality is in the sense of real causation and mutual necessity exists, such that each is original in one respect and derivative in another, not in a purely causal relationship. It is known that the removal of ownership of the slave's person is a pure cause for the removal of ownership of pleasure, and not an effective cause (illa) in it. For often the removal of ownership of the slave's person occurs without there being any removal of ownership of pleasure, as in the case of neck-exempted slaves, or the freeing of female slaves who are mahram to the manumitter, or married female slaves, because there is no ownership of pleasure in these situations to be removed by the removal of ownership of the slave's person. In summary: The removal of ownership of the slave's person, although it is a cause for the removal of ownership of pleasure, the second removal is not intended from it. And the removal of ownership of pleasure, although it is a necessary consequence of the removal of ownership of the slave's person, the removal of ownership of the slave's person is not a necessary consequence of the removal of ownership of pleasure. So it is not valid for majaz to occur here from the other side. It is only valid to use the name of the cause, which is manumission, for its effect, but not the opposite, such that manumission occurs by the word 'talaq', whether explicit or metaphorical. If you say: It should occur by the word 'talaq' with intention (niyyah). I reply: Intention is only considered after the validity of using the word in its metaphorical meaning. Here there is no validity of using the word 'talaq' for manumission, neither literally nor metaphorically. So intention is irrelevant, just as when one intends 'earth' from the word 'sky'. Then know that the school of al-Shafi'i might be justified by saying that it is possible to utter 'talaq' and intend manumission by way of isti'ara (metaphor) based on the relation of similarity, not by way of majaz mursal with a causal relationship. Hence, it would be said that majaz from both sides is not valid. The response to this is: Sometimes it is said that isti'ara is not valid with any description, because it is definitely impossible to use 'sky' for 'earth' despite their sharing existence and origination. Rather, a well-known attribute that has special connection to the source of the metaphor is necessary, like the bravery of the lion. This is not realized in talaq and itq. For manumission (itq) is the establishment of a special power (al-quwwa al-makhsusa), while divorce (talaq) is removal of a restriction (al-qayd). There is no similarity between the two meanings in the aspect for which they were legislated.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the legal effect of using the word for manumission (itq) to effect divorce (talaq) and vice versa. The author, in the Hanafi tradition, first establishes the theory of majaz mursal, distinguishing between cases where the relationship between original and derivative is bilateral (each is both original and derivative in some respect) versus unilateral. In bilateral cases, metaphor can run in both directions; in unilateral, only one direction. The author then applies this to the relationship between manumission and divorce. Manumission removes ownership of the person (ra'aba), and divorce removes ownership of marital pleasure (mut'a). Since removal of the latter is a necessary consequence of the former (a freed slavewoman cannot remain married without consent), manumission is the cause and divorce the effect. However, the causal relationship is purely one-way: manumission causes divorce, but divorce does not cause manumission. Indeed, divorce often occurs without any freeing of a slave (e.g., when a free woman is divorced). Therefore, the metaphorical use is valid only from cause to effect: saying 'I free you' can mean divorce (by majaz), but saying 'I divorce you' cannot mean manumission. The Hanafi position (shared by Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad) is that divorce does occur with the word itq with the intent of divorce, but manumission does not occur with the word talaq, even with intent, because the metaphorical relation is not valid. The Shafi'i school holds that manumission can occur with talaq. The passage refutes this by noting that even if one tries to base the metaphor on similarity (isti'ara) rather than causality, there is no common attribute between manumission (bestowing power) and divorce (removing a restriction) that would justify the metaphor. The author emphasizes that intention cannot validate a usage that is not linguistically or legally sound. Thus, the practical takeaway is a clear asymmetry: in Hanafi law, a husband who says to his slavegirl wife 'I free you' intending divorce will have his marriage dissolved; but if he says 'I divorce you' intending to free her, no manumission occurs.

Key Terms
المجاز المرسل (al-majāz al-mursal): A type of figurative speech where a word is used to mean something other than its original meaning based on a non-similarity relation like causality, inherence, etc., and the meaning is a necessary consequence of the original.
السببية المحضة (as-sababiyya al-maḥḍa): A purely causal relationship where the cause does not necessarily entail the effect in every instance, and there is no reciprocal necessity.
العلة الغائية (al-illa al-ghāʾiyya): The final cause; the purpose for which something is done. In the text, it refers to the effect being the intended goal of the cause, making the relationship reciprocal in terms of origin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man says to his slave wife 'I divorce you' (طلقتك) intending to free her, does the manumission occur?
A) Yes, because the intention (niyyah) makes the word effective.
B) Yes, because both divorce and manumission involve ending a relationship.
C) No, because the metaphorical use of 'talaq' for 'itq' is not valid; intention cannot rectify an invalid linguistic use.
D) No, but the manumission occurs by way of metaphor if the husband explicitly intends it.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the metaphor from manumission to divorce is valid (cause to effect), but the reverse is not, so saying 'I divorce you' cannot effect manumission.

Talaq — Divorce

Condition: Ownership at Time of Ta'liq
Clarifies a condition for valid conditional emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that a slave must be owned at the time of ta'liq for the emancipation to be valid, and apply it to identify valid and invalid scenarios.

Core Idea
For conditional emancipation, the slave must be owned when the condition is stated.

In Hanafi law, a conditional suspension (ta'liq) can be used for emancipation or divorce. However, one essential requirement is that the subject (e.g., a slave) must already be in the ownership of the person making the statement at the moment the condition is uttered. This short passage from the commentary clarifies this rule, drawing a direct parallel with divorce. Understanding this condition prevents confusion about when such conditions actually take effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
The passage states the act is 'ta'liq' (suspending emancipation on a condition) and the condition is that the slave must be in the owner's possession at the time of the statement.
The condition is about the time of utterance, not the time the condition is fulfilled.
2
Understand why this condition matters
Without ownership at the time of ta'liq, the suspended statement is attached to a non‑existent or unowned subject, making the condition ineffective. This prevents potential abuse.
The same principle applies to divorce: if a man says 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced' before marriage, it is invalid.
3
Apply to a scenario
Consider: A man says 'If Zaid enters, my slave 'Amr is free.' At that moment he does not own 'Amr. Later he buys 'Amr and then Zaid enters. The condition fails because 'Amr was not owned at the time of ta'liq.
The condition is not retroactive; ownership must be present at the time of utterance.
4
Distinguish from other forms
This ruling differs from a statement like 'Any slave I acquire will be free' because that is a ta'liq on future ownership, not a specific existing slave. The text distinguishes the two.
The passage itself assumes you already know the divorce ruling, so we note the parallel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لكن يشترط أن يكون العبد في ملكه وقت التَّعليق، كما عرفت في الطلاق.
He said: But it is condition that the slave be in his ownership at the time of suspension, as you have known in divorce.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This is a ruling_statement specifying a precondition for a conditional emancipation (ta'liq bi al-shart). If a person says 'If so-and-so enters, my slave is free' but he does not own a slave at that moment, later when he acquires a slave, the condition is not applied retroactively; the slave is not freed. The same principle applies in divorce: if a man says 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced' before marriage, it is not valid. This is because the suspension is attached to a non-existent subject at the time of utterance. The Hanafi school requires the subject (the slave) to be in his ownership at the time of the ta'liq for it to be effective. This prevents potential abuse and ensures clarity.

Key Terms
تعليق بالشرط (ta'liq bi al-shart): Suspending an emancipation or divorce on a future condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says: 'If Zaid enters this house, then my slave 'Amr is free.' At that time, the man does not own 'Amr. Later he buys 'Amr, and then Zaid enters. According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what happens?
A) 'Amr is free because the condition was fulfilled after ownership.
B) 'Amr is not free because he was not owned at the time of the statement.
C) 'Amr is free only if the man intended it.
D) 'Amr becomes free only if the man says the statement again after buying him.

Key Takeaway
For a conditional emancipation (ta'liq) to be valid, the slave must be owned by the speaker at the moment the condition is stated.

Dower Fractions in Ambiguous Divorce with Three Wives
Commentary on dower fractions in ambiguous divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the dower fractions for each of the three wives in the ambiguous divorce case and explain the reasoning behind each fraction using the conceptual analysis of the two utterances.

Core Idea
Dower fractions depend on the possible intentions behind the ambiguous divorces, not equal division.

This lesson unpacks a detailed Hanafi ruling on dower when a husband ambiguously divorces two of his three wives before consummation, then dies without clarification. The fractions (3/4, 5/8, 7/8) are derived by analyzing which wives could have been intended in each utterance. Understanding this reasoning strengthens your skill in applying legal logic to property rights in family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the three wives
Read the passage and identify the three wives: the one who left (kharijah), the one who stayed (thabitah), and the one who entered (dakhilah). Note the two utterances: first to two wives, second to the remaining two.
The husband dies without clarifying his intentions.
2
State the dower fractions for each wife
From the text, extract the fractions: kharijah gets 3/4, thabitah gets 5/8, dakhilah gets 7/8. Write them down.
These fractions are of their respective dowers.
3
Analyze the first utterance's effect on dower
The first utterance 'one of you is divorced' applies to kharijah and thabitah. It validly divorces one of them, so half the dower of the pair is forfeited. This half is split equally between them, so each loses a quarter of her dower. Thus kharijah loses 1/4, thabitah loses 1/4.
The first utterance is valid regardless of which one was intended.
4
Analyze the second utterance's effect on dower
The second utterance applies to thabitah and dakhilah. It is valid only if the first utterance intended kharijah; if it intended thabitah, the second is invalid. Since we don't know, we consider both possibilities and the minimum necessary deduction: the second utterance causes a loss of 1/8 from each of thabitah and dakhilah (since the quarter forfeited if valid is split between them).
This yields a total loss for thabitah of 1/4+1/8=3/8, so she keeps 5/8. Dakhilah loses only 1/8, so she keeps 7/8.
5
Confirm the fractions are independent of equal dowers
The text notes that the condition 'dowers are equal' is incidental; the fractions apply even if dowers differ. Each wife's fraction is a proportion of her own dower.
This ensures the ruling is universally applicable.

Source Text

(1) أي مات بلا بيان ففي المسألة أحكام ثلاثة : المهر والميراث والعدّة أما حكم الميراث فللداخلة نصف والنصف بين الخارجة والثابتة نصف وعلى كل منهن عدة الوفاة احتياطاً. ينظر: «مجمع الأنهر» (١: ٥٢٣)

الصفة المذكورة )
وللثابتة خمسة أثمان المهر ؛ لأنه سقط من مهرها بالإيجاب الأول الربع لها لما ذكرنا. وبالإيجاب الثاني الثمن ؛ لأنّ الإيجاب الثاني صحيح في حال إن أريد بالإيجاب ا الأول الخارجة غير صحيح في حال إن أريد بالإيجاب الأول الثابتة، ولما كان صحيحاً في حال دون حال يسقط به الربع ، موزعاً بين الثابتة والداخلة، فيسقط من مهر كلّ واحدةٍ الثمن، فكان الساقط من مهر الثابتة بالإيجابين ثلاثة أثمان ، وبقي لها خمسة أثمان، وقد الداخلة بالإيجاب الثاني الثمن، وبقي لها سبعة أثمان.
سقط من مهرا
وأما حكم الميراث فللداخلة نصفُ الميراث، والنصف الآخر بين الخارجة والثابتة نصفان؛ لأنَّ الداخلة وارثة بيقين، ولا يزاحهما إلا امرأة واحدة ؛ لأنَّ إحدى الأخريين مطلقة بيقين بالإيجاب الأوّل، فإن أريد بالإيجاب الأول الثابتة ، بطل الإيجاب الثاني، فكانت الداخلة وارثة.
وإن أريد بالإيجاب الأول الخارجة، فالإيجاب الثاني دائر بين الثابتة والداخلة ، وليست إحداهما أولى من الأخرى، فكان الميراث بينهن نصفين، فكـيـف مـا كـان فالداخلة وارثةٌ ،بيقين ولا يزاحمها إلا امرأة واحدة، فالنصفُ لها، والنصف الآخر بين الأخريين نصفين، وعلى كلّ واحدٍ منهنّ عدة الوفاة ؛ لاحتمال كونهما منكوحة، ولا تجب عدة الطلاق بيقين ؛ لعدم الدخول. انتهى.
6
[١]قوله : مهرهن على السواء ؛ هذا القيد اتفاقي، فإنّ سقوط ربع مهر الخارجة، وثمن الداخلة وثلاثة أثمان الثابتة على كل تقدير، سواءً كان مهرهن متساوياً أو مختلفاً. [٢] قوله : على الصفة المذكورة ؛ فحينئذ يؤمر الزوج بالبيان، فإن بدأ ببيان الإيجاب الأول وقال : عنيت الخارجة طلّقت هي، وصح الإيجاب الثاني، فيؤمر بالبيان ؛ لكونه دائراً بين الثابتة والداخلة، فتطلّق التي يعنيها، وإن قال : عنيت بالإيجاب الأول الثابتة، طلقت هي وبطل الإيجاب الثاني ؛ لكونه دائراً بين المطلقة وغير المطلقة. وإن بدأ ببيان الإيجاب الثاني فإن قال : عنيت به الداخلة، طلقت هي، وبقي الأول دائراً بين الثابتة والخارجة، فيؤمر بالبيان، فتطلّق أيتهما عينها، وإن قال : عنيت
Book of Emancipation: Ambiguous Emancipation. And if he divorces likewise before consummation, the quarter of the dower of the one who left falls, and three-eighths of the one who stayed, and one-eighth of the one who entered. And from the one who stayed half of it, which is twenty-one, and from the one who entered one-sixth of it, which is seven, so the total shares of emancipation according to the two opinions are forty-two, which is one-third of the wealth, and the shares of earning (sa'ayah) are eighty-four, which is two-thirds of the wealth. (And if he divorces likewise before consummation), the quarter of the dower of the one who left falls, and three-eighths of the one who stayed, and one-eighth of the one who entered. (5): He is emancipated from the one who left to the extent of one-third of forty-two, which is fourteen, for its sixth is seven, and the third is double the sixth, and he must earn the rest, i.e., the amount of two-thirds, which is twenty-eight. [1] His saying: half of it; because the emancipated from him is three out of four, and after 'awl from six, so he is emancipated to the amount of twenty-one dirhams, and he must earn the remaining half. [2] His saying: its sixth; because the emancipated from him is one share, which is one-sixth relative to six, so he is emancipated to the amount of seven dirhams, and he must earn five-sixths of his value, which is thirty-five dirhams. [3] His saying: and the shares of earning; meaning the shares that the three slaves earn for the heirs. [4] His saying: likewise; meaning he said addressing his two wives: 'One of you is divorced,' so one left and a third entered, then he said addressing the first who stayed and the third who entered: 'One of you is divorced,' and he did not clarify what he meant by his ambiguity in the two utterances. [0] His saying: and one-eighth of the one who entered; Abu al-Qasim Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-'Attabi said in his commentary on the book 'al-Ziyadat' by Muhammad: Here there are two rulings: the ruling of dower and the ruling of inheritance. As for the ruling of dower, the one who left gets three-quarters of the dower; because with the first utterance half of the dower falls between the one who left and the one who stayed, so from the dower of the one who left a quarter falls and three-quarters remain. (1) i.e., he died without clarification, so in the matter there are three rulings: dower, inheritance, and 'iddah. As for the ruling of inheritance, the one who entered gets half, and the other half is between the one who left and the one who stayed equally, and each of them must observe the 'iddah of death as a precaution. See: 'Majma' al-Anhur' (1:523). Meaning if he had three wives whose dowers were equal, and he divorced them before consummation in the described manner. And for the one who stayed five-eighths of the dower; because from her dower fell by the first utterance the quarter, as we mentioned. And by the second utterance the eighth; because the second utterance is valid in the case where the first utterance intended the one who left, and invalid in the case where the first utterance intended the one who stayed, and since it is valid in one case but not in another, a quarter falls by it, distributed between the one who stayed and the one who entered, so from the dower of each one an eighth falls. So the amount that fell from the dower of the one who stayed by the two utterances is three-eighths, and five-eighths remain for her. And from the dower of the one who entered fell by the second utterance one-eighth, and seven-eighths remain for her. As for the ruling of inheritance, the one who entered gets half of the inheritance, and the other half is equally between the one who left and the one who stayed; because the one who entered is definitely an heir, and she is only rivaled by one woman; because one of the other two is definitely divorced by the first utterance. If the first utterance intended the one who stayed, the second utterance is invalid, so the one who entered is an heir. And if the first utterance intended the one who left, then the second utterance is ambiguous between the one who stayed and the one who entered, and neither is more entitled than the other, so the inheritance is half between them. So in any case, the one who entered is definitely an heir and is only rivaled by one woman, so half is for her, and the other half is equally between the other two. And each of them must observe the 'iddah of death due to the possibility of being a wife, and the 'iddah of divorce is not definitely required due to lack of consummation. End. [1] His saying: their dowers are equal; this condition is incidental, for the falling of a quarter of the dower of the one who left, an eighth of the one who entered, and three-eighths of the one who stayed occurs in every case, whether their dowers are equal or different. [2] His saying: in the described manner; then the husband is ordered to clarify. If he begins by clarifying the first utterance and says: 'I meant the one who left,' she is divorced, and the second utterance becomes valid, so he is ordered to clarify because it is ambiguous between the one who stayed and the one who entered, so whichever he means is divorced. If he says: 'I meant by the first utterance the one who stayed,' she is divorced and the second utterance is invalid because it becomes ambiguous between a divorced woman and a non-divorced woman. If he begins by clarifying the second utterance and says: 'I meant the one who entered,' she is divorced, and the first remains ambiguous between the one who stayed and the one who left, so he is ordered to clarify, and whichever he specifies is divorced. If he says: 'I meant'
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on a complex case of ambiguous divorce (الطلاق المبهم) involving three wives. The case: A man has three wives with equal dowers. He says to two of them, 'One of you is divorced,' causing one to leave and a third to enter. Then he says to the first (who stayed) and the third (who entered), 'One of you is divorced.' He then dies without clarifying his intentions. The commentators explain the rulings on dower (mahr) and inheritance. For dower: The wife who left receives three-quarters of her dower; the wife who stayed receives five-eighths; the wife who entered receives seven-eighths. These fractions are derived from analyzing the two utterances and their possible interpretations. For inheritance: The wife who entered receives half of the inheritance (since she is definitely a wife), and the remaining half is divided equally between the other two wives. All wives must observe the 'iddah of death (four months and ten days) as a precaution because it is possible that each is a widow. The 'iddah of divorce is not required because there was no consummation. The passage also discusses the procedure for the husband to clarify his intentions if alive. The condition that dowers are equal is incidental; the fractions for dower remain the same even if dowers differ. The Hanafi position is presented without mention of other schools.

Key Terms
خارجة (al-kharijah): The wife who left after the first ambiguous divorce utterance.
ثابتة (al-thabitah): The wife who stayed (the first wife who was not told to leave initially).
داخلة (al-dakhilah): The wife who entered (the third wife introduced after the first utterance).
عتق مبهم (al-'itq al-mubham): Ambiguous emancipation; the chapter title but the text deals with divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of ambiguous divorce with three wives, what fraction of the mahr does the wife who stayed (الثابتة) receive?
A) 1/4
B) 3/8
C) 5/8
D) 7/8

Key Takeaway
The fractions of dower in ambiguous divorce are determined by the logical possibilities of the husband's intentions, ensuring each wife receives her rightful share.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Inheritance and Iddah in Ambiguous Divorce with Three Wives
Commentary on inheritance and iddah in ambiguous divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the inheritance shares and iddah obligations for each wife in the ambiguous divorce case, and explain the reasoning based on certainty of being a wife.

Core Idea
Certainty of being a wife at death determines inheritance; all wives observe iddah of death as precaution.

This lesson continues the analysis of the ambiguous divorce case, focusing on inheritance and iddah. The wife who entered is definitely a wife, so she inherits half. The other two share the other half equally, because one of them is definitely divorced. All three must observe the iddah of death (four months and ten days) as a precaution, since it is possible each is a widow. Understanding this reinforces the Hanafi principle that certainty (yaqin) overrides doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key difference: inheritance vs. dower
Dower is based on the effect of the utterances on each wife; inheritance is based on the certainty of being a wife at the time of death. Recognize that the entering wife (dakhilah) is definitely a wife, while the other two have doubt.
The text explicitly states: 'the one who entered is definitely an heir (warithah bi-yaqin).'
2
Determine the inheritance share of the entering wife
Since dakhilah is definitely a wife, she inherits. She is rivaled in inheritance only by one other wife (because one of the other two is definitely divorced). Therefore, she gets half of the inheritance.
The rival is the one wife from the other pair who is not divorced.
3
Determine the inheritance share of the other two wives
The remaining half is divided equally between the other two (kharijah and thabitah). Because one of them is definitely divorced, only one of them actually inherits, but since we don't know which, the half is split equally between them.
The text says: 'the other half is between the two of them equally.'
4
Understand the reasoning behind the iddah
All three wives must observe the iddah of death (4 months and 10 days) because it is possible that each of them is a widow. The iddah of divorce is not required because there was no consummation.
The iddah of death is precautionary (ihtiyatan).
5
Summarize the three rulings (mahr, inheritance, iddah)
From the passage: dower fractions, inheritance shares (half for dakhilah, quarter each for others), and iddah of death for all. This covers all three rulings mentioned.
As cited in Majma' al-Anhur.

ولو طلق كذلك قبل وطءٍ سَقَطَ ربعُ مهرِ مَن خَرَجَت، وثلاثة أثمانِ مَن ثبتت ، وتُمْنُ مَن دخلَتْ
ومن الثَّابِتِ نصفه، وهو واحد وعشرون، ومن الداخل سدسه، وهو سبعة، فمجموع سهام العتق على القولين اثنان وأربعون، وهو ثلث المال، وسهام السعاية" أربعة وثمانون، وهي ثلثا المال (ولو طلق كذلك قبل وط ) سقط ربع مهرٍ مَن خَرَجَت ، وثلاثةُ المانِ

[2] His saying: in the described manner; then the husband is ordered to clarify. If he begins by clarifying the first utterance and says: 'I meant the one who left,' she is divorced, and the second utterance becomes valid, so he is ordered to clarify because it is ambiguous between the one who stayed and the one who entered, so whichever he means is divorced. If he says: 'I meant by the first utterance the one who stayed,' she is divorced and the second utterance is invalid because it becomes ambiguous between a divorced woman and a non-divorced woman. If he begins by clarifying the second utterance and says: 'I meant the one who entered,' she is divorced, and the first remains ambiguous between the one who stayed and the one who left, so he is ordered to clarify, and whichever he specifies is divorced. If he says: 'I meant'
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on a complex case of ambiguous divorce (الطلاق المبهم) involving three wives. The case: A man has three wives with equal dowers. He says to two of them, 'One of you is divorced,' causing one to leave and a third to enter. Then he says to the first (who stayed) and the third (who entered), 'One of you is divorced.' He then dies without clarifying his intentions. The commentators explain the rulings on dower (mahr) and inheritance. For dower: The wife who left receives three-quarters of her dower; the wife who stayed receives five-eighths; the wife who entered receives seven-eighths. These fractions are derived from analyzing the two utterances and their possible interpretations. For inheritance: The wife who entered receives half of the inheritance (since she is definitely a wife), and the remaining half is divided equally between the other two wives. All wives must observe the 'iddah of death (four months and ten days) as a precaution because it is possible that each is a widow. The 'iddah of divorce is not required because there was no consummation. The passage also discusses the procedure for the husband to clarify his intentions if alive. The condition that dowers are equal is incidental; the fractions for dower remain the same even if dowers differ. The Hanafi position is presented without mention of other schools.

Key Terms
خارجة (al-kharijah): The wife who left after the first ambiguous divorce utterance.
ثابتة (al-thabitah): The wife who stayed (the first wife who was not told to leave initially).
داخلة (al-dakhilah): The wife who entered (the third wife introduced after the first utterance).
عدة الوفاة (iddat al-wafat): The waiting period of four months and ten days required for a widow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the ambiguous divorce case, what is the inheritance share of the entering wife (الداخلة)?
A) 1/3
B) 1/2
C) 1/4
D) 7/8

Key Takeaway
The inheritance share and iddah in ambiguous divorce are determined by the certainty of being a wife at death, not by the sequence of events.

Fractional Mahr Distribution in Ambiguous Triple Divorce
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr fractions in ambiguous divorce with death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the mahr reduction for the excluded, confirmed, and incoming wives in an ambiguous triple-wife divorce before consummation, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn on the incoming wife's loss.

Core Idea
In ambiguous divorce before consummation, the mahr is halved and re-halved, but Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn differ on the incoming wife's loss: 1/8 vs 1/4.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in Hanafi family law: a husband ambiguously divorces two of his three wives with two pronouncements before consummation, then one wife dies before clarification. How is the mahr distributed? The answer involves applying the principle of tanṣīf (halving) and reveals an important intra-madhhab disagreement between Imam Muhammad and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). We will work through the fractions step by step and understand the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the three wives
There are three wives: the one not included in the first pronouncement (excluded), the wife included in both pronouncements (confirmed), and the wife included only in the second pronouncement (incoming). The first pronouncement is addressed to the excluded and confirmed; the second to the confirmed and incoming. Both are before consummation, so the first pronouncement makes one wife irrevocable (ba'in).
The ambiguity: we do not know which wife was intended in each pronouncement.
2
Calculate the distribution according to Imam Muhammad
First pronouncement: half the dower of one wife falls, but since it is ambiguous, that half is shared equally between the two addressed (excluded and confirmed), so each loses 1/4 of her dower. Second pronouncement: the remaining half of the dower of one wife is at stake, but again ambiguous between confirmed and incoming, so half of that (1/4) falls, shared equally, so each loses an additional 1/8. Total: excluded loses 0 (never hit by second), confirmed loses 3/8 (1/4+1/8), incoming loses 1/8.
Imam Muhammad treats the confirmed as still eligible for the second pronouncement, analogous to the manumission case.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's distribution
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, after the first pronouncement the confirmed wife is irrevocably divorced and thus cannot be a locus for the second pronouncement. Therefore the second pronouncement applies entirely to the incoming wife, causing half of her dower to fall? Actually the second pronouncement drops half of the remaining half (1/4) which falls entirely on the incoming, so she loses 1/4 of her dower. The confirmed loses only the first 1/4, and excluded loses nothing.
This is the same as the manumission analogy according to them.
4
Compare the underlying reasoning
The dispute hinges on whether the confirmed wife remains eligible for the second pronouncement. Imam Muhammad argues yes, so the second pronouncement is distributed between confirmed and incoming. The Sahibayn argue no, because the first pronouncement made her irrevocably divorced, so she is out; therefore the second pronouncement exclusively targets the incoming. This leads to different fractions.
The commentary in the source provides detailed analogy to manumission to justify the difference.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Practical consequence: if the incoming wife's dower is 100 dirhams, Imam Muhammad gives her a reduction of 12.5 dirhams, while the Sahibayn give 25 dirhams. The text does not specify the mufta bihi, but later Hanafi practice follows Imam Muhammad's view.
The difference is significant in financial terms; the mufti must know which position is operative.
